Word had gotten around somehow that it was Joash's own son, Gideon, who had done the deed. ["Then the men of the city said unto Joash, Bring out thy son, that he may die"]{.class-0} (vs. 30). Like any good Catholic, they were ready to "string up" Gideon.

But those Baalites underestimated Gideon's father. Joash wasn't a very good Baal worshipper. He may have been going along with it for one reason or another, but he wasn't going to be buffaloed by a bunch of religious fanatics. He told that mob, "Hey, man, if Baal is the right God like you've been telling me, let [him]{.class-1} do something about it; it's [his]{.class-1} problem."

That's good thinking. I sat across from a couple of Moslems one time in my office. I had just gotten through telling them exactly what I thought of their "prophet." One of those Mohammedans said, "If you were in my country, you'd be [killed]{.class-1} for saying that." I said, "I know. That's because your god is an impotent weakling." He said, "What do you mean?" I said, "Your god is so weak, he has to have somebody else do his killing for him. If my God wanted you dead, He'd drop you before you could walk out that door."

Listen, people, if you don't like my preaching, can't stand my Bible, and blaspheme my Saviour, I'm not going to kill you. I'll pray for you and try to win you to Christ, but if you turn down the Lord Jesus Christ, I'll move on to someone else who'll listen. God doesn't need me to do His "dirty work" for Him. If He wants you dead for rejecting His Son or blaspheming His name, all He has to do is touch you once in the heart or the lungs, and you'll be "pushing up daisies."

Joash must have had a sense of humor, for he gives Gideon a "nickname": ["Jerubbaal"]{.class-0} (vs. 32). The word means ["Let Baal plead against him."]{.class-0} It was a slap in the face to every one of those Baal worshipers. Every time they saw Gideon after that, they were reminded by the name Jerubbaal of how impotent their god was. Gideon was still walking around alive, and Baal hadn't (and couldn't) do anything about it.

33 Then all the Midianites and the Amalekites and the children of the east were gathered together, and went over, and pitched in the valley of Jezreel.

34 But the Spirit of the LORD came upon Gideon, and he blew a trumpet; and Abiezer was gathered after him.

35 And he sent messengers throughout all Manasseh; who also was gathered after him: and he sent messengers unto Asher, and unto Zebulun, and unto Naphtali; and they came up to meet them.

36 And Gideon said unto God, If thou wilt save Israel by mine hand, as thou hast said,

37 Behold, I will put a fleece of wool in the floor; and if the dew be on the fleece only, and it be dry upon all the earth beside, then shall I know that thou wilt save Israel by mine hand, as thou hast said.

38 And it was so: for he rose up early on the morrow, and thrust the fleece together, and wringed the dew out of the fleece, a bowl full of water.

39 And Gideon said unto God, Let not thine anger be hot against me, and I will speak but this once: let me prove, I pray thee, but this once with the fleece; let it now be dry only upon the fleece, and upon all the ground let there be dew.

40 And God did so that night: for it was dry upon the fleece only, and there was dew on all the ground.

Verse 33 is a "red flag" that what you are about to read is a type of the Second Advent. There are ["the kings of the east"]{.class-0} (Rev. 16:12). ["The valley of Jezreel"]{.class-0} is the southern end of the Valley of Megiddo, where the Battle of Armageddon will be fought.

We have already covered the Holy Spirit coming on people (vs. 34) under our comments on 3:10, which see. The ["trumpet"]{.class-0} is another indication that you are reading of the Second Advent in type (see our comments on 3:27).

["Abiezer"]{.class-0} was one of the six families of the tribe of Manasseh that lived on the west side of the Jordan (Josh. 17:2). As the family of Gideon, they joined him first, then the rest of the Manassites (vs. 35). Once again, Zebulun and Naphtali figure prominently into the scenario of the Second Advent (see our comments on 5:18), but this time they are joined by Asher, which tribe was missing in the battle between Barak and Sisera (see 5:17 and comments).

Now having received a sign that it was God who had called him to deliver Israel (vss. 17--21), and with the Spirit of the Lord on him (vs. 34), look at what Gideon says in verse 36: ["IF thou wilt save Israel by mine hand, as thou hast said."]{.class-0} [He's still doubting!]{.class-1} But God is patient with him, as He is with us many a time. And with all his doubts, the New Testament still puts Gideon in the "Hall of Faith" (Heb. 11:32) (see our remarks at the end of chap. 4 about how the New Testament deals with the sins of Old Testament saints).

Here, Gideon "puts out the fleece"---literally. A fleece, of course, is a coat of wool taken from a sheep. Gideon takes a woolen fleece and puts it out overnight. The "test" of God's will, first, is for the dew to be on the fleece only and not on the surrounding ground. God answers Gideon's request, and the next morning Gideon wrings a whole bowl of water out of the fleece, but the ground is bone dry.

Gideon, though, figures he asked for the wrong thing. After all, if this were merely a natural occurrence, then the most likely thing to happen would be for the fleece to soak up all the water (not really, but that is how an infidel would think). So Gideon reverses it and asks for God to make the fleece dry and the ground wet, and of course, that is exactly what happened. Knowing human nature, if Gideon thought he could have gotten away with a couple of more "tests" to get out of fighting the Midianites and Amalekites, I am sure he would have done it, for after the sign and the tests, the ["mighty man of valour"]{.class-0} was still scared to death (see 7:10).

Now there are some lessons here for us as New Testament believers in determining God's will. The first would be, we should not tempt God over the will of God as clearly revealed in the word of God. There is no need to "pray over" whether you should live a clean life as a Christian since ["this is the will of God, even your sanctification, that ye abstain from fornication"]{.class-0} (1 Thess. 4:3). You don't have to ["try the spirits"]{.class-0} (1 John 4:1) when it comes to praising God in the midst of any situation He puts you since: ["In every thing give thanks: for this is the will of God in Christ Jesus concerning you"]{.class-0} (1 Thess. 5:18). There's no need to "put out the fleece" when it comes to living right because God told you point blank that was His will (1 Pet. 2:14--15). When it comes to a clear command of God, like being a witness for Jesus Christ (Acts 1:8), you don't have to wonder if it's the will of God.

Usually, though, with a Christian who loves the Lord, believes the Book, and is trying to live right, those general truths aren't the problem. "The devil is in the details," as the saying goes: do I take this job, whom should I marry, where do I go to school, does God want me on the mission field---those kinds of things.

If you want to know the will of God in matters like that, here is how to proceed.

1\. [Judge everything by the Scriptures.]{.class-1} David prayed, ["give me understanding ACCORDING TO THY WORD" ]{.class-0}(Psa. 119:169). If it's against the clear revelation of God in His written words, [don't do it.]{.class-1} You don't need to pray over anything God told you to do or not to do.

2\. [Trust God.]{.class-1} Live by faith. The Bible says, ["without faith it is impossible to please him]{.class-0} \[God\]["]{.class-0} (Heb. 11:6). God won't always give you a "sign"; sometimes you have to step out by faith and let God lead you one step at a time.

3\. [Stay in fellowship with God.]{.class-1} The Lord is not going to reveal His will to a disobedient child of God (Eph. 5:1--17).

4\. [Pray about it.]{.class-1} James said, ["If any of you lack wisdom, let him ASK OF GOD, that giveth to all men liberally, and upbraideth not; and it shall be given him"]{.class-0} (James 1:5). Sometimes, a Christian can't figure out the will of God in a matter because he hasn't asked God about it---["ye have not, because ye ask not"]{.class-0} (James 4:2). If you ask, and [ask with persistence,]{.class-1} you will get an answer (see our comments on Luke 11:9--10 in that Commentary).

Now, suppose you have done all of that, and you still don't know. That would be an extreme case, I'll grant you, but such cases do happen. If I were in the word on a regular basis, walking by faith, staying in fellowship with God, praying about the matter, and still had no clear leadership on the thing, only then would I "put out the fleece." I would ask the Lord for some kind of confirmation about what to do; nothing crazy or wild, mind you, like a "miracle," but a definite indication.

The trouble at that point is, you have to be willing to follow up on what the Lord indicates or confirms. Don't pray, "Lord, I'll do this if you'll just show me," and then don't do it when He shows you. Ecclesiastes 5:5 says it's better not to vow than to ["vow and not pay"]{.class-0}; you would be better off not promising anything than to make a promise you have no intention of keeping if the outcome is something you don't like. If you start making excuses for not following through on what you promised, you blew it. The way to proceed is to be neutral so that no matter what the Lord shows you, you go in that direction. Your own desires and preferences should have no bearing on the matter.

You say, "What if the Lord is testing me?" Then He will fix the thing. If the Lord is just trying to see if you will live by faith and things start going the wrong way, He will block you like He did Abraham with the ram there in Genesis 22:10--13.

Bob Jones Sr. had something to say about that. He said, "Give God your heart, and He will straighten out the kinks in your head." If God has your heart (Prov. 23:26), He will overrule the mistakes you make and work them out for your good (Rom. 8:28).

[]{#section-0_1_a010297b2b8ec859_7091.xhtml}

Chapter 7

7:1 Then Jerubbaal, who is Gideon, and all the people that were with him, rose up early, and pitched beside the well of Harod: so that the host of the Midianites were on the north side of them, by the hill of Moreh, in the valley.

2 And the LORD said unto Gideon, The people that are with thee are too many for me to give the Midianites into their hands, lest Israel vaunt themselves against me, saying, Mine own hand hath saved me.

3 Now therefore go to, proclaim in the ears of the people, saying, Whosoever is fearful and afraid, let him return and depart early from mount Gilead. And there returned of the people twenty and two thousand; and there remained ten thousand.

4 And the LORD said unto Gideon, The people are yet too many; bring them down unto the water, and I will try them for thee there: and it shall be, that of whom I say unto thee, This shall go with thee, the same shall go with thee; and of whomsoever I say unto thee, This shall not go with thee, the same shall not go.

5 So he brought down the people unto the water: and the LORD said unto Gideon, Every one that lappeth of the water with his tongue, as a dog lappeth, him shalt thou set by himself; likewise every one that boweth down upon his knees to drink.

6 And the number of them that lapped, putting their hand to their mouth, were three hundred men: but all the rest of the people bowed down upon their knees to drink water.

7 And the LORD said unto Gideon, By the three hundred men that lapped will I save you, and deliver the Midianites into thine hand: and let all the other people go every man unto his place.

8 So the people took victuals in their hand, and their trumpets: and he sent all the rest of Israel every man unto his tent, and retained those three hundred men: and the host of Midian was beneath him in the valley.

Now back in chapter 6, Gideon blew the trumpet to gather the people of Abiezer, the rest of Manasseh, Asher, Zebulun, and Naphtali to him to battle. He has an army of 32,000 men (vs. 3), more than the 10,000 the Lord allotted to Barak in 4:6, 10. Yet look at what the Lord says in verse 2: ["The people that are with thee are too many for me to give the Midianites into their hands . . . ."]{.class-0} Why? [". . . lest Israel vaunt themselves against me, saying, Mine own hand hath saved me."]{.class-0} If you add up the numbers in 8:10, you see Gideon is about to take on a force of 135,000 with less than a quarter of that number, and God says it's too many.

The principle here is: ["Not by might, nor by power, but by my spirit, saith the LORD of hosts"]{.class-0} (Zech. 4:6). Jonathan said to his armorbearer one time, ["There is no restraint to the LORD to save by many or by few"]{.class-0} (1 Sam. 14:6).

The danger of numbers is that you tend to trust in them. You figure the more you have, the better chance you have (see Christ's words in Luke 14:31--32). But God doesn't want you trusting in your own strength; He wants you trusting [Him.]{.class-1}

["Some trust in chariots, and some in horses: but we will remember the name of the LORD our God"]{.class-0} (Psa. 20:7).

["There is no king saved by the multitude of an host: a mighty man is not delivered by much strength . . . . ]{.class-34}[Our soul waiteth for the LORD: he is our help and our shield. For our heart shall rejoice in him, because we have trusted in his holy name"]{.class-0} (Psa. 32:16, 20--21).

The reason God judged David so harshly for numbering Israel in 2 Samuel 24 was because He didn't want David boasting about how big an army he had. That's the idea behind ["lest Israel vaunt themselves against me."]{.class-0} It's: "Look at us! Look at how big our army is! Look at how strong we are! We don't need God; we can do it on our own!"

Once more the picture here is of the Second Advent. Isaiah says of the day of Christ's return:

["The lofty looks of man shall be humbled, and the haughtiness of men shall be bowed down, and the LORD alone shall be exalted in that day"]{.class-0} (Isa. 2:11).

So Gideon has 32,000 men, and God says, "That's too many." To whittle the number down, the Lord has Gideon do two things. The first thing is send everyone home who is scared. That was a rule given in the Law.

["And the officers shall speak further unto the people, and they shall say, What man is there that is fearful and fainthearted? let him go and return unto his house, lest his brethren's heart faint as well as his heart"]{.class-0} (Deut. 20:8).

Patton wrote once, "Cowardice is epidemic." One fellow turns tail and runs in battle, and his buddy sees that and figures, "I'm not going to stay here and take it," so he runs too. Two more see the second guy, and they follow suit. Pretty soon you have a rout on your hands. Cowardice is epidemic.

The Lord said, "If you're scared, go home. I don't want you disheartening your buddy in the fight." That's what happened with the ten spies who brought the evil report back from Canaan (Deut. 1:28).

Here, Gideon gives his men the chance to go home if they're scared, and he loses [22,000 of them]{.class-1}---two-thirds of his army GONE, just like that! As a pastor, it makes me wonder sometimes what I have in my congregation. Gideon could only count on [a third]{.class-1} of his bunch.

With 22,000 of his original army gone, he is left with 10,000---the same number Barak had. But even then, the Lord says it is too many (vs. 4), so God gives Gideon a test to weed out some more. Gideon marched his 10,000 remaining men down to the river and told them to get a drink. That seems like a foolish thing; after all, what can drinking water have to do with killing Midianites and Amalekites? But there was a reason God had Gideon do that.

Nine thousand, seven hundred of those men got down on their knees and bent down and sucked up the water; the other 300 dipped the water in their hands and lapped it with their tongues like a dog (hence the term ["dog-face"]{.class-1} for a WWII infantryman and [dog-tags]{.class-1} worn by the military in every successive generation since then. You won't get by a [King James]{.class-1} Bible. It will tell you what to do, and you will do it). God said, ["By the three hundred men that lapped will I save you"]{.class-0} (vs. 7).

Now why is that? Well, the Bible says, ["All the labour of man is for his mouth, and yet the appetite is not filled"]{.class-0} (Eccl. 6:7). Paul said about a certain bunch, ["For they that are such serve not our Lord Jesus Christ, but their own belly"]{.class-0} (Rom. 16:18); and ["whose God is their belly"]{.class-0} (Phil. 3:19). Those men who got down on their knees to slurp the water up only had one thing in mind: filling their gut with water---getting the most water in the shortest time possible.

The Lord has a "method to this madness." You take those 300 that lapped like dogs. Do you know what they were doing? They were bringing the water up to their mouths so they could keep track of what was going on around them. They were alert; they were looking out for the enemy. You can't see anything around you when you're on your knees with your head stuck in the water. If the enemy had attacked right then, 9,700 of those fellows would have been caught unawares. God didn't want any of those men who weren't looking for a fight.

God wants to see if you will pay attention to what He said. When Elisha lay dying, he had Joash, the King of Israel, come in to him. When Joash came to see the dying prophet, Elisha said, "Get your bow and arrows." Joash had his bow and arrows in his hand, and Elisha put his hand on the King's hand and said, ["Shoot"]{.class-0} (2 Kings 13:17). As the King shot an arrow out the window toward the east where Syria was located, Elisha said, "That's ['The arrow of the LORD'S deliverance . . . from Syria']{.class-0}" (2 Kings 13:17).

Then Elisha had Joash take the rest of the arrows in his hand and strike the ground with them. The King struck the ground three times and stopped. Elisha said, "You fool! You fool! You fool!"

"What do you mean 'You fool'?" asked Joash in consternation.

Elisha said, "You only struck the ground three times."

"Well, you never told me how many times to hit the ground," replied the King.

"Didn't I tell you those arrows were ['The arrow of the LORD'S deliverance . . . from Syria']{.class-0}?" said Elisha. "Now you're only going to whip Syria three times instead of beating them into the ground until they couldn't recover."

What was Joash's problem? He wasn't paying attention to what God said. He thought Elisha was just a crazy old man talking.

I'll give you another example. At the Last Supper, Jesus tells the disciples, "All of you are going to forsake me tonight." Peter says, "Not me, Lord. All these other guys might desert you, but I won't." Jesus told Peter, "Even you, Peter, because ['it is written]{.class-0} \[Scripture!\][, I will smite the shepherd, and the sheep of the flock shall be scattered abroad']{.class-0}" (Matt. 26:31).

But Peter said, ["Though all men shall be offended because of thee, yet will I never be offended"]{.class-0} (Matt. 26:33). In other words, "I know what the Scriptures say, Lord, but the Scriptures made a mistake. They actually [meant]{.class-1} (haven't we heard that one before?!) all the sheep [except me."]{.class-1}

What was the matter? Peter paid no attention at all to Jesus when He said, ["it is written."]{.class-0} He forgot what he heard Jesus say back on the Mount of Olives:

["Heaven and earth shall pass away, BUT MY WORDS SHALL NOT PASS AWAY"]{.class-0} (Matt. 24:35).

Now Gideon is left with 300 men. That's 300 against 135,000. Each Israelite is up against 450 of the enemy. If any of you are going out into the ministry, you are going to be up against the graduates of Bob Jones, Tennessee Temple, Crown, Pensacola Christian, Maranatha, BBC, Hyles Anderson, Moody, Wheaton, Fuller, Pacific Coast, and the Seminaries at Dallas, Denver, Louisville, and New Orleans. Your odds will be just about as good as Gideon's men. You will be going up against men who don't believe the Book, who correct the Book, and who attack the Book. They will hate your guts and fight you every step of the way. And that's not counting the government, the IRS, the HRS, the liquor industry, the sex perverts, the abortionists, etc. But you can make it with the Lord's help.

To encourage myself in this regard, I have an inscription in German at the front of all my Bibles and notebooks. In English it means, "Don't forget Pips Priller." Who was Pips Priller? He was a German fighter pilot in World War II. He was called "Pips" because he was a short fellow. When the Allies invaded Normandy on D-Day, Pips was ordered to strafe the beach. His squadron had been sent off to the Russian front, and all he had was his wingman. Together they went up against 13,000 aircraft. He came down through the anti-aircraft fire, the balloon nets, and the wire hangings and saw those 13,000 aircraft plus ships; and he said, "Man, what a sight!"

Pips and his wingman made their run on the beach, and they flew so low that the GIs on the beach could see their faces. One of them said he would know Pips anywhere after the war. Then Pips flew back up through the 13,000 aircraft and back to Germany. He died in 1961.

Now listen, brethren, when you're up against the opposition, the temptation will be to throw in the towel and quit. [Don't quit!]{.class-1} As far as I know, Pips Priller wasn't even a saved man. If an unsaved German working for a demoniac dictator can face odds like two against 13,000 and survive, what are  you worried about? Don't you have a better leader than Pips Priller? I think so. Doesn't He have your welfare in mind? I believe so (Rom. 8:28). You probably won't ever have to face the odds Priller had to face, so how come you can't survive?

9 And it came to pass the same night, that the LORD said unto him, Arise, get thee down unto the host; for I have delivered it into thine hand.

10 But if thou fear to go down, go thou with Phurah thy servant down to the host:

11 And thou shalt hear what they say; and afterward shall thine hands be strengthened to go down unto the host. Then went he down with Phurah his servant unto the outside of the armed men that were in the host.

12 And the Midianites and the Amalekites and all the children of the east lay along in the valley like grasshoppers for multitude; and their camels were without number, as the sand by the sea side for multitude.

13 And when Gideon was come, behold, there was a man that told a dream unto his fellow, and said, Behold, I dreamed a dream, and, lo, a cake of barley bread tumbled into the host of Midian, and came unto a tent, and smote it that it fell, and overturned it, that the tent lay along.

14 And his fellow answered and said, This is nothing else save the sword of Gideon the son of Joash, a man of Israel: for into his hand hath God delivered Midian, and all the host.

The Lord orders Gideon to go down against the Midianite host, and the promise is:[ "for I have delivered it into thine hand"]{.class-0} (vs. 9). But evidently, Gideon doesn't move, for God goes on to say, ["But if thou fear to go down . . ."]{.class-0} (vs. 10). The ["mighty man of valour"]{.class-0} is still scared stiff, because he takes the Lord up on His offer.

With his servant Phurah, he sneaks down to the outer edges of the Midianite camp, and Gideon hears a couple of GIs talking. One fellow tells his buddy, "Man, I had a weird dream. I dreamed this loaf of barley bread came tumbling down the hill into our camp. It smacked into a tent and knocked it down flat."

His buddy replied, "Why, don't you know what that dream means? That ['cake of barley bread']{.class-0} is ['the sword of Gideon the son of Joash']{.class-0} (vs. 14). His God has delivered our whole host ['into his hand']{.class-0}."

Look at that thing. Gideon was typified in that dream by barley bread, the very cheapest kind of bread that the poorest of the people had. We are reminded of Gideon's words to the Angel of the Lord:[ "my family is poor in Manasseh, and I am the least in my father's house"]{.class-0} (6:15).

But the bread isn't just Gideon; it's ["the SWORD of Gideon."]{.class-0} The bread is connected with the sword. That's a picture of the word of God.

["Man shall not live by bread alone, but by every word that proceedeth out of the mouth of God"]{.class-0} (Matt. 4:4).

["For the word of God is quick, and powerful, and sharper than any twoedged sword"]{.class-0} (Heb. 4:12).

So once more you have a picture of the Second Advent. When Christ returns at the Battle of Armageddon, ["the bread of life"]{.class-0} (John 6:35) has ["a sharp sword"]{.class-0} going ["out of his mouth"]{.class-0} (Rev. 19:15).

Now the word has gotten out to the Midianites and Amalekites about Gideon; they even know who his daddy is (vs. 14). The Midianites and Amalekites are completely demoralized by what they heard.

The Lord has no problem messing up armies. One time in 2 Kings 7, the Syrians were routed because God made them to hear a great noise of chariots, horses, and a large army when there was no cavalry or infantry there. The Syrians thought the Hittites were attacking when they weren't. There is no telling what the Lord put into the heads of the Midianites and the Amalekites about Gideon to make them so scared of him.

15 And it was so, when Gideon heard the telling of the dream, and the interpretation thereof, that he worshipped, and returned into the host of Israel, and said, Arise; for the LORD hath delivered into your hand the host of Midian.

16 And he divided the three hundred men into three companies, and he put a trumpet in every man's hand, with empty pitchers, and lamps within the pitchers.

17 And he said unto them, Look on me, and do likewise: and, behold, when I come to the outside of the camp, it shall be that, as I do, so shall ye do.

18 When I blow with a trumpet, I and all that are with me, then blow ye the trumpets also on every side of all the camp, and say, The sword of the LORD, and of Gideon.

19 So Gideon, and the hundred men that were with him, came unto the outside of the camp in the beginning of the middle watch; and they had but newly set the watch: and they blew the trumpets, and brake the pitchers that were in their hands.

20 And the three companies blew the trumpets, and brake the pitchers, and held the lamps in their left hands, and the trumpets in their right hands to blow withal: and they cried, The sword of the LORD, and of Gideon.

21 And they stood every man in his place round about the camp: and all the host ran, and cried, and fled.

22 And the three hundred blew the trumpets, and the LORD set every man's sword against his fellow, even throughout all the host: and the host fled to Bethshittah in Zererath, and to the border of Abelmeholah, unto Tabbath.

Once Gideon hears the interpretation, he no longer has any doubts or fears about what God has called him to do. God has not only demoralized the enemy, He has put courage and strength into the heart of His commander. Gideon is finally the ["mighty man of valour"]{.class-0} the Lord said he was.

If I have said it once, I've said it a thousand times: "The most important thing in the world for you as a Christian is for you to maintain fellowship with God." Everything depends on that. The Germans have a saying: "Everything depends on God's blessing." Bob Jones Sr. said, "One man and God are a majority." All that means that if you stay in fellowship with the Lord, when the time comes, He will encourage and strengthen you. He will go ahead of you and mess your enemies up and prevent them from doing what they are going to do. And it doesn't matter how many enemies you have, you win and they lose!

Until you get to Heaven, you will never know how many times God overruled the Devil on your behalf. You'll get up there, and the Lord will show you all the times when things were planned and plotted against you, and all the things that could have gone wrong, and God screwed them up so they couldn't be carried out.

If you are going to do something for God, there will be somebody out to get you. You had better pray with David:

["Deliver me not over unto the will of mine enemies"]{.class-0} (Psa. 27:12).

["Deliver me from the hand of mine enemies, and from them that persecute me"]{.class-0} (Psa. 31:15).

["O deliver me from the deceitful and unjust man"]{.class-0} (Psa. 43:1).

Similar prayers are found in Psalm 59:1--2, 71:4, 119:134, 140:1, 142:6, 143:9, and 144:11.

Note Gideon's response to the interpretation:[ "he worshipped"]{.class-0} (vs. 15). Gideon is thanking God for what God has shown him.

Now the Table of Organization (TO) for Gideon's 300 was three companies of 100 each. The standard military unit is composed of a forward unit, a flanking unit, and a reserve unit---[three.]{.class-1}

The Table of Equipment (TE) is a pitcher (an earthenware jug with a handle on it), a lamp, and a trumpet---no sword. The lamp was lit, but it was covered up with the pitcher.

The three groups of 100 are stationed at different points on the ridge overlooking the valley where the Midianites and Amalekites are encamped. When Gideon blows his trumpet, each man is to blow his trumpet as well and shout, ["The sword of the LORD, and of Gideon"]{.class-0} (vs. 18). Here is another picture of the Second Advent, where the deliverer is directly equated with Jehovah. That is exactly the case of Jesus Christ at the Second Advent (see Zech. 12:8--10 and our remarks in that Commentary).

After blowing the trumpet in his right hand, each man is to take the lamp covered with the pitcher in his left hand and bust the pitcher and "let his little light shine." Of course, if you are not familiar with how ancient armies worked, the effectiveness of this maneuver is not readily apparent. After all, why would 135,000 Midianites and Amalekites flee from 300 Jews with trumpets, broken pitchers, and lamps?

But you see, each light represented a regiment or battalion of troops. Those Midianites and Amalekites would be awakened by 300 trumpet blasts. When they ran out of their tents to see what was going on, they would see they were surrounded by 300 lights, each light, in their minds, representing 200--800 men. The Midianites and Amalekites thought they were being attacked by anywhere from 60,000--240,000 men. No wonder the Midianites were in a panic.

Note in verse 19 ["the middle watch."]{.class-0} In the Old Testament, the nighttime was divided into three watches of four hours each: the evening watch from 6:00 p.m.--10:00 p.m., the midnight watch from 10:00 p.m.--2:00 a.m., and the morning watch from 2:00 a.m.--6:00 a.m. In the New Testament, the night is divided into four watches of three hours each (Mark 13:35): the evening watch from 6:00 p.m.--9:00 p.m., the midnight watch from 9:00 p.m.--12:00 a.m., the ["cockcrowing"]{.class-0} watch from 12:00 a.m.--3:00 a.m., and the morning watch from 3:00 a.m.--6:00 a.m. Here, the nighttime is a picture of the Tribulation, so ["the beginning of the middle watch"]{.class-0} would be about half way through the Tribulation and would match the "midnight watch" of the wise and foolish virgins in Matthew 25:6. It is a picture of a rapture of Jewish saints at some point before the Battle of Armageddon.

Now Gideon has gone from a frightened bunny rabbit to a veteran General. He makes a "reconnaissance" (vss. 9--14). He utilizes the elements of encirclement and surprise (vss. 16--21), and he pursues the adversary and annihilates them (chap. 8). What caused such a transformation? It was[ God working through him:]{.class-1} the Lord made him a ["mighty man of valour."]{.class-0}

["Look on me, and do likewise"]{.class-0} (vs. 17). That's the principle by which Patton operated: be an [example]{.class-1} to the troops; don't just tell them what to do. That has always been my position. I [show]{.class-1} my students what to do. If I tell them to preach on the street, then I am going to be out there preaching on the street. I don't know of any other way to handle it.

Verse 22:[ "And the three hundred blew the trumpets, and the LORD set every man's sword against his fellow, even throughout all of the host: and the host fled."]{.class-0} In the camp of the Midianites, it was 10:00. Taps had been sounded, and the troops were bedded down and just drifting off to sleep. The guard had just been switched. Suddenly, 300 trumpets sound off. The Midianites and Amalekites hop out of their tents, rubbing the sleep from their eyes, and trying to pull on their trousers. They look up on the ridge surrounding their camp and see the lights of 300 battalions bearing down on them. They "lose it" and grab their swords and begin slashing at the nearest person. [They start killing each other!]{.class-1}

That is a picture of Armageddon. The Lord said, ["In that day . . . I will smite every horse with astonishment, and his rider with madness"]{.class-0} (Zech. 12:4). He said, ["the riders on horses shall be confounded"]{.class-0} (Zech. 10:5).

Zechariah 14:13 says,[ "And it shall come to pass in that day, that a great tumult from the LORD shall be among them; and they shall lay hold every one on the hand of his neighbour, and his hand shall rise up against the hand of his neighbour."]{.class-0}

Haggai 2:22 says, ["And I will overthrow the throne of kingdoms, and I will destroy the strength of the kingdoms of the heathen; and I will overthrow the chariots, and those that ride in them; and the horses and their riders shall come down, every one by the sword of his brother."]{.class-0}

At the Battle of Armageddon, God takes the minds of 200 million UN troops. He strikes the horses with blindness (Zech. 12:4), and the troops turn their guns and missiles on each other.

The Lord is a great trickster, did you know that? He pulls tricks on people to mess them up when they don't listen to Him. The Bible says, ["the foolishness of God is wiser than men"]{.class-0} (1 Cor. 1:25). I say, "The Lord has got a monkey wrench that will fit any nut in this country."

When the Lord wants to mess someone up, He doesn't have to exert Himself. In Genesis 11, all He had to do was confound the languages. When He wanted to mess up a bunch of puffed-up, egotistical scholars, He let a woman give birth to a baby with no man involved.

Years ago, there was a young Ensign in the navy who considered himself somewhat of an intellectual. He had come out to church and heard my testimony. After the service, he came out to a fellowship in a member's home, and he struck up a conversation with me. He considered me an "Elmer Gantry" who didn't believe what I was preaching. He said to me, "Nobody could read what you've read and believe what you believe."

I said, "If you think I haven't read what I've read, just give me a little examination on Plato, Socrates, Aristotle, Nietzsche, and the rest of them and see if I have read them."

"Well I don't doubt that you have read them," he replied. "But you could not believe what you believe having read what you have read."

I decided I would settle his hash right there and then, so I said to him, "You profess to be an honest fellow, don't you?"

"Yes," he said.

"You are one of those fellows that tells it like it is, right?"

Again he replied, "Yes."

"You wouldn't let any false humility or tact or diplomacy prevent you from telling the truth, would you?"

"No," he said.

"Then I want to ask you three questions," I said, "and when I'm done, I want you to ask me the same three questions. Will you answer me honestly and then ask me the same three questions?"

"Yes," he said.

I said, "Okay. First, have you got a good mind?"

"Yes," he said, "I have an excellent mind." That was an honest answer from that fellow; there was no false humility about him.

"Second," I said, "who gave it to you?"

He said, "It came with the equipment." He was an atheist, see. He didn't believe anything he had came from God.

I went on: "Three, how long do you think you will have a good mind?"

He said, "I will have a good mind as long as I live."

I said, "Now ask me the same three questions."

"I know what you'll say," he replied.

"Yes, but ask me anyway."

So he said, "Do you have a good mind?"

"Yes," I said, "I have a really good one."

He hesitated, and I said, "Second question."

He said, "I know what you'll say."

I said to him, "Don't give me that jazz. Don't back out now, man. You said you would ask me, so ask me."

"All right," he said, "who gave you your mind?"

"God," I said, "and God could take it from me in twenty seconds and leave me a raving maniac."

When I said that, that Ensign's face turned just as white as a sheet. He got up and said, "Excuse me." He called himself a taxi and left. I found out later that he had been under psychiatric treatment for about two months. [He was losing his mind.]{.class-1}

["And the host fled to Bethshittah in Zererath, and to the border of Abelmeholah, unto Tabbath"]{.class-0} (vs. 22). Most Bible atlases locate these places on the east side of Jordan, and this is probably the case. Look at verse 24: Gideon is trying to enlist Ephraim's help in taking the ford of the Jordan at Bethbarah.

23 And the men of Israel gathered themselves together out of Naphtali, and out of Asher, and out of all Manasseh, and pursued after the Midianites.

24 And Gideon sent messengers throughout all mount Ephraim, saying, Come down against the Midianites, and take before them the waters unto Bethbarah and Jordan. Then all the men of Ephraim gathered themselves together, and took the waters unto Bethbarah and Jordan.

25 And they took two princes of the Midianites, Oreb and Zeeb; and they slew Oreb upon the rock Oreb, and Zeeb they slew at the winepress of Zeeb, and pursued Midian, and brought the heads of Oreb and Zeeb to Gideon on the other side Jordan.

After the Midianite host had been routed with 300 men following God's battle plan, the men of Naphtali, Asher, and Manasseh join in the pursuit of the Midianites. Undoubtedly there were some of the men sent home in chapter 6. Now that there could be no doubt that God had been the one to give the victory over the Midianites and Amalekites, they are allowed to participate in the "mopping up" operation.

The Midianite host flew from the Valley of Jezreel in the north towards the fords of Jordan in the south. Gideon sent messengers to the Ephraimites to "head 'em off at the pass." ["Bethbarah"]{.class-0} was where the two spies crossed the Jordan; it was where Joshua and the children of Israel crossed miraculously into Canaan; and it is where the Lord Jesus was baptized. You can read the connection of all these things to the path of Christ's Second Advent under our remarks on Joshua 2:8 in that Commentary. So once more this account is marked as a type of the Second Advent.

Now if there is any doubt that this is an account, in type, of the Second Advent, look at ["Oreb and Zeeb"]{.class-0} in verse 25. The cross-reference to the verse is Isaiah 10:24--26.

["Therefore thus saith the Lord GOD of hosts, O my people that dwellest in Zion, be not afraid of the Assyrian ]{.class-0}\[historically Sennacherib, but a prophetic reference to the Antichrist\][: he shall smite thee with a rod, and shall lift up his staff against thee, after the manner of Egypt ]{.class-0}\[Pharaoh, another type of the Antichrist\][. For yet a very little while ]{.class-0}\[a reference to the Tribulation---see our comments on John 16:16 in that Commentary\][, and the indignation shall cease, and mine anger in their destruction. And the LORD of hosts shall stir up a scourge for him]{.class-0} \[the Antichrist\][ according TO THE SLAUGHTER OF MIDIAN AT THE ROCK OREB."]{.class-0}

In speaking of the Moslem confederation to come against Israel in the last days, the psalmist asks God to: ["Do unto them as unto the Midianites . . . Make their nobles like Oreb, and like Zeeb: yea, all their princes as Zebah, and as Zalmunna"]{.class-0} (Psa. 83:9, 11).

Oreb and Zeeb are two Hebrew words. Oreb means "raven," and Zeeb means "wolf." The raven is an unclean bird (Lev. 11:15) that ends up in the lake of fire in Edom in the Millennium (Isa. 34:11). In Matthew 7:15, ["false prophets"]{.class-0} are likened to ["ravening wolves."]{.class-0} So what it looks like here is the slaying of Oreb and Zeeb is a picture of the overthrow of the Antichrist and the False Prophet at the Battle of Armageddon (Rev. 19:20).

[]{#section-0_1_14e480259baa0e5a_7092.xhtml}

Chapter 8

8:1 And the men of Ephraim said unto him, Why hast thou served us thus, that thou calledst us not, when thou wentest to fight with the Midianites? And they did chide with him sharply.

2 And he said unto them, What have I done now in comparison of you? Is not the gleaning of the grapes of Ephraim better than the vintage of Abiezer?

3 God hath delivered into your hands the princes of Midian, Oreb and Zeeb: and what was I able to do in comparison of you? Then their anger was abated toward him, when he had said that.

The Ephraimites become angry that they weren't called to the battle, but even if they had been, the likelihood is that the Lord would have sent them home. After all, God whittled down Gideon's original army from 32,000 to 300.

Ephraim is evidently predisposed to this kind of behavior, for they do the same thing in Judges 12:1 when Jephthah went after the Ammonites. They don't fare as well in that chapter, though, as they do here. Here, Gideon follows the advice of Proverbs 15:1 and appeases their egos. He flatters them and turns away their anger: ["What have I done now in comparison of you?"]{.class-0} (vs. 2); ["what was I able to do in comparison of you?"]{.class-0} (vs. 3). Jephthah doesn't put up with their lip. He fights against them and kills them for their arrogance (12:4--6).

We have seen this monumental arrogance and egotism before back in Joshua 17:14--18. Ephraim wanted special favors without having to do the work to get them. The problem, according to God in Hosea 11:12, 12:1, was that they were [liars.]{.class-1} In 12:2 here in Judges, Jephtah [had called them,]{.class-1} and they [refused to come]{.class-1}. More than likely, that is exactly what happened in the case of Gideon.

If you want to apply the passage spiritually, Ephraim stands in type for a Christian who is never in on the real action, but when the real action shows up, he gets in on the tail end of it so he can get credit for it. Such people always want to be patted on the back, flattered, and praised for every move they make.

4 And Gideon came to Jordan, and passed over, he, and the three hundred men that were with him, faint, yet pursuing them.

5 And he said unto the men of Succoth, Give, I pray you, loaves of bread unto the people that follow me; for they be faint, and I am pursuing after Zebah and Zalmunna, kings of Midian.

6 And the princes of Succoth said, Are the hands of Zebah and Zalmunna now in thine hand, that we should give bread unto thine army?

7 And Gideon said, Therefore when the LORD hath delivered Zebah and Zalmunna into mine hand, then I will tear your flesh with the thorns of the wilderness and with briers.

8 And he went up thence to Penuel, and spake unto them likewise: and the men of Penuel answered him as the men of Succoth had answered him.

9 And he spake also unto the men of Penuel, saying, When I come again in peace, I will break down this tower.

Notice that all the "tag alongs" from Naphtali, Asher, and Manasseh (7:23) don't follow Gideon over Jordan. And if Ephraim was so eager to get in the fight, why didn't they go over Jordan with Gideon to take down the Midianite Kings? Gideon is left with the 300 with whom he started. You can see now why God sent a bunch of them home: they weren't willing to see the fight to the end. They went up to the Jordan and quit; they got the glory and quit.

Now Gideon and his "300" are worn out; they are about to drop on their feet. They come to the city of Succoth, and Gideon doesn't ask for the men of Succoth to join in the fight. He only asks for bread to strengthen his 300 men in their pursuit.

The leaders of Succoth, though, turn Gideon down: ["Are the hands of Zebah and Zalmunna now in thine hand, that we should give bread unto thine army?"]{.class-0} (vs. 6). They figure Gideon hasn't won the battle yet, and they don't want to face recrimination from the Midianites should Gideon lose.

But Gideon had something the men of Succoth didn't have:[ the promise of God.]{.class-1} Gideon was now so confident that he would kill the Midianites and Amalekites down to the last man that he was ready to punish any Jew who stood in his way or wouldn't help. Gideon's promise to the "city council" of Succoth was: ["Therefore when the LORD hath delivered Zebah and Zalmunnah into mine hand, then I will tear your flesh with the thorns of the wilderness and with briers"]{.class-0} (vs. 7).

At this point, the Hebrew scholars just can't resist temptation, and they have to tell you that the Hebrew word for ["tear"]{.class-0} actually means "to thresh." Having told you that, what do they do with it? Here is Adam Clarke:

"What this punishment consisted in I CANNOT SAY \[i.e., complete, total ignorance and blindness from the "original Hebrew"\]; it must mean a severe punishment \[you think?!\]: as if he had said, I will thresh your flesh with briers and thorns, as corn is threshed out with threshing instruments."

Now do you know what ["I will tear your flesh with the thorns of the wilderness and with briers"]{.class-0} means? It means Gideon took thorns and briers that grew in the wilderness and beat the men of Succoth with them until their backs were torn to shreds. In speaking of the scourging of Christ, the psalmist said, ["The plowers plowed upon my back: they made long their furrows"]{.class-0} (Psa. 129:3).

How hard was that to understand? Did you really need the "original Hebrew" to tell you something that you [couldn't say]{.class-1} (see Clarke's comments above) when the English text in verses 7 and 16 told you exactly what to say?

Having followed the "original Hebrew" into pitch-black darkness, Clarke missed the real "nuggets" in the English text. Now we will go to the Hebrew and Greek here, but look how differently we do it as opposed to those who correct the [Authorized]{.class-1} text to exalt their own authority in the eyes of their students (see our work [How to Teach the Original Greek]{.class-1}).

When you get down to verse 16 where Gideon fulfills what he said in verse 7, the text says:[ "And he took the elders of the city, and thorns of the wilderness and briers, and with them he TAUGHT the men of Succoth."]{.class-0}

The Hebrews word for ["taught"]{.class-0} is [yada]{.class-1} and means "causing to know something" or "education," but it means to teach through [punishment.]{.class-1} The New Testament equivalent is found in Hebrews 12:5--11. It is [paideia]{.class-1} and refers to [chastening.]{.class-1}

So when Ephesians 6:4 says fathers are to bring up their children ["in the nurture and admonition of the Lord,"]{.class-0} that word ["nurture"]{.class-0} is [paideia.]{.class-1} According to Hebrews 12:6, it means to "teach with a [whip."]{.class-1}

You have two generations of Americans who have not learned [anything]{.class-1} growing up because they never learned that [disobedience brings suffering and pain.]{.class-1} This has produced in our country the largest jail population of any country in the world.

I asked a warden of a prison one time what all the inmates had in common. He told me that none of them had ever experienced pain for doing wrong. Coming up, momma and daddy couldn't spank them lest they be accused of "child abuse." They couldn't be paddled in school when they did wrong. When they broke the law, the police couldn't take the billy club to them; that would be "police brutality." If they experienced any pain at all, it was from the "gang." So they ended up in prison where the gang still controlled them behind bars. No wonder this country is in the mess it is in.

Back to Judges 8. Gideon goes on to Penuel and asks them for bread, and the leaders turn him down for the same reason Succoth did. But Gideon's patience has worn out. He doesn't plan to "teach" the men of Penuel anything. He is going to tear down the tower of the city where everyone would flee in an attack (vs. 9). And when Gideon finally does that, he kills all the men in the city (vs. 17).

10 Now Zebah and Zalmunna were in Karkor, and their hosts with them, about fifteen thousand men, all that were left of all the hosts of the children of the east: for there fell an hundred and twenty thousand men that drew sword.

11 And Gideon went up by the way of them that dwelt in tents on the east of Nobah and Jogbehah, and smote the host: for the host was secure.

12 And when Zebah and Zalmunna fled, he pursued after them, and took the two kings of Midian, Zebah and Zalmunna, and discomfited all the host.

13 And Gideon the son of Joash returned from battle before the sun was up,

14 And caught a young man of the men of Succoth, and enquired of him: and he described unto him the princes of Succoth, and the elders thereof, even threescore and seventeen men.

15 And he came unto the men of Succoth, and said, Behold Zebah and Zalmunna, with whom ye did upbraid me, saying, Are the hands of Zebah and Zalmunna now in thine hand, that we should give bread unto thy men that are weary?

16 And he took the elders of the city, and thorns of the wilderness and briers, and with them he taught the men of Succoth.

17 And he beat down the tower of Penuel, and slew the men of the city.

Now these are ["Zebah and Zalmunna"]{.class-0} (vs. 10), the two Kings of the Midianites (vs. 5). When Gideon said to the Ephraimites, ["God hath delivered into your hands the princes of Midian, Oreb and Zeeb: and what was I able to do in comparison of you?"]{.class-0} (vs. 3), at that moment he might not have been able to brag about killing anybody important among the Midianite host, but he is about to be able to. He is about to take down two men more important than ["Oreb and Zeeb."]{.class-0} The Ephraimites contented themselves with second best; Gideon went after the ["perfect, will of God"]{.class-0} (Rom. 12:2; see our comments on that verse in the [Bible Believer's Commentary on Romans]{.class-1}).

Notice in verse 10 that the total number of the Midianite host was 135,000. One hundred-twenty thousand were slain by themselves (7:22), by Gideon and his 300 men, by the men of Naphtali, Asher, and Manasseh (7:23), and by the Ephraimites (7:24--25) before the host ever crossed Jordan. The host has been reduced to only 15,000.

What was left of the Midianite host are now encamped at Karkor, located by the Oxford Bible Atlas east of Moab, far south of Bethbarah. The Midianite Kings are over-confident: ["for the host was secure"]{.class-0} (vs. 11). They figured they were so far away from Israel that they no longer had anything to fear. And if it had been up to the men of Naphtali, Asher, Manasseh, and Ephraim, the Midianites would have been right. But they didn't count on Gideon's tenacity. Gideon wasn't just opportunistic like the Naphtalites, Asherites, and Manassites; he wasn't out for personal glory like the Ephraimites; he wasn't even out for freedom from Midianite oppression. Gideon was operating on [the promise of God]{.class-1} that the [whole]{.class-1} host of Midian would be delivered into his hand. Remember that in your Christian warfare. If you are going to ["war a good warfare,"]{.class-0} you have to operate on the word of God (the Scriptures), not on your own personal gain or glory.

Operating on what God said to him, Gideon was able to take on 135,000 men with only a force of 300 and send them packing. Now with the same 300, exhausted and famished, he takes on 15,000 and [whips them.]{.class-1}

Gideon takes[ "Zebah and Zalmunnah"]{.class-0} alive, not so he can have a "war trophy" like Saul did in 1 Samuel 15, but to show the men of Penuel and Succoth that what God said was so.

Verse 13:[ "And Gideon the son of Joash returned from battle before the sun was up."]{.class-0} So the whole battle and pursuit took place between 10:00 at night and 6:00 in the morning. That is what is known as a "forced march." The old saying attributed to Nathan Bedford Forrest, a Confederate cavalry commander during the Civil War, when asked how he won so many battles was: "I get there the furstest with the mostest." To chase and slaughter a host of 135,000 from the Valley of Jezreel to a location east of Moab in eight hours took some "hoofing it," I kid you not. Of course, the Hebrew scholars don't believe it could be done, so they want to change the translation to "returned from the ascent of Chares." But by now you know our opinion of such infidelity, and you know where to consign such a translation.

We have already commented on verses 15--17 under verses 7--9. The elders of Succoth whom Gideon taught "readin', 'ritin,' and 'rithmetic . . . to the tune of a hickory stick" were 77 in number. The men of Penuel were not so lucky; they all "got it in the neck."

18 Then said he unto Zebah and Zalmunna, What manner of men were they whom ye slew at Tabor? And they answered, As thou art, so were they; each one resembled the children of a king.

19 And he said, They were my brethren, even the sons of my mother: as the LORD liveth, if ye had saved them alive, I would not slay you.

20 And he said unto Jether his firstborn, Up, and slay them. But the youth drew not his sword: for he feared, because he was yet a youth.

21 Then Zebah and Zalmunna said, Rise thou, and fall upon us: for as the man is, so is his strength. And Gideon arose, and slew Zebah and Zalmunna, and took away the ornaments that were on their camels' necks.

Now comes the time when Gideon deals with ["Zebah and Zalmunnah."]{.class-0} He asked them a question which you cannot locate in the text of Judges. There's nothing in Judges 6 about the Midianites killing anybody, but evidently they killed Gideon's actual brothers (["the sons of my mother"]{.class-0}---vs. 19). Mt. Tabor is located on the eastern end of the Valley of Jezreel where the Midianites had been encamped (6:33).

With the battle over, Gideon is now arrayed like a leader, probably in a ["coat of many colours"]{.class-0} (see our remarks under 5:30), for the two Kings identify him and his brothers as ["children of a king"]{.class-0} (vs. 18). You might infer from this that before the Midianites and Amalekites came along, Gideon's family was not as poor as they were in 6:15.

In verses 19--21, Gideon is exercising his right as a ["revenger of blood"]{.class-0} (see Num. 35). Normally if a soldier kills someone in war, it is not counted as murder, but in this case, the Midianites came in unprovoked and murdered Gideon's brothers. So Gideon is avenging his brothers' deaths.

At first, Gideon orders his oldest boy, Jether, to kill the Kings, but Jether is young with no combat experience. Killing a man for the first time is a scary thing; blood and guts are gory. A lot of those soldiers in the Civil War and World War I, in their first battle, ran without firing a shot. They had never killed a man before and were scared. Can you imagine having to hack a man to death like Samuel did in 1 Samuel 15:33? A kid like Jether just didn't have it in him yet.

So ["Zebah and Zalmunna"]{.class-0} ask Gideon to do it because ["as the man is, so is his strength"]{.class-0} (vs. 21). There are two reasons for that request. The first thing is that Gideon is now an experienced warrior; he knows how to kill a man quickly and cleanly. The second is the Oriental sense of honor. That is hard for Americans to understand, but in Oriental culture, it is a privilege to be killed by a real man. It is an insult to be killed by a child or a woman (see Abimelech's reaction in 9:54).

Gideon slays the two Midianite Kings and takes the ornaments on their camels' necks as the spoil. Ornaments on the camels were a sign of one's social class and riches. The Kings' camels would have been especially "decked out."

22 Then the men of Israel said unto Gideon, Rule thou over us, both thou, and thy son, and thy son's son also: for thou hast delivered us from the hand of Midian.

23 And Gideon said unto them, I will not rule over you, neither shall my son rule over you: the LORD shall rule over you.

24 And Gideon said unto them, I would desire a request of you, that ye would give me every man the earrings of his prey. (For they had golden earrings, because they were Ishmaelites.)

25 And they answered, We will willingly give them. And they spread a garment, and did cast therein every man the earrings of his prey.

26 And the weight of the golden earrings that he requested was a thousand and seven hundred shekels of gold; beside ornaments, and collars, and purple raiment that was on the kings of Midian, and beside the chains that were about their camels' necks.

27 And Gideon made an ephod thereof, and put it in his city, even in Ophrah: and all Israel went thither a whoring after it: which thing became a snare unto Gideon, and to his house.

The first rumblings by the Israelites for a King arise in verse 22. The reason they wanted a King was because they didn't want God ruling over them (1 Sam. 8:5--7, 10:19). At this point, a monarchy could have been set up by a "democratic vote," for the Israelites were ready to make Gideon their King. They wanted to be out from under God's dominion and be under the dominion of a sinner like themselves.

But Gideon had more sense than that. He refused to be King. He said, ["the LORD shall rule over you"]{.class-0} (vs. 23). You have another example in history like that. When Oliver Cromwell had to deal with the Catholic traitor in the monarchy (Charles I), he was offered the crown of England himself. Cromwell was a true believer in Jesus Christ who put a Geneva Bible in the hands of all of his troops. He joined Joshua and David as the only three Generals in history who never lost a battle. When offered the crown of England, he turned it down and told the Parliament, "The Lord God shall rule over you."

Now Gideon made the right choice when it came to turning down the kingship, but what he does next is a big mistake that plagues him and his household. He asked for all the golden earrings off the ears of the Midianite army that had been taken as spoil. When Aaron did that, Israel fell into idolatry with the golden calf (Exod. 32). Something similar is about to take place here (vs. 27).

Notice, in verse 24, the connection between the Midianites and the Ishmaelites. We have run into this before in Genesis 37:25, 28, 36 when Joseph's brothers sold him into slavery. The rejected sons of Abraham were in alliance together (see our comments on 6:3).

Of course the people have no problem giving Gideon what he wants for saving them from the Midianites, so they turn over to him 1,700 shekels of gold earrings, not counting all the ornaments, collars, and chains on the Kings and their camels. In today's gold market, the earrings alone would be worth \$85,000.

The ["ephod"]{.class-0} o[f verse 26 is like a T-shirt, and it is used for worship (e.g., Exod. 28:4; Judg. 18:14, 17;]{.class-75} 1 Sam. 2:28). If you put it on its side and attached it to a pole, you would have a flag. What Gideon has made is a large flag or banner, decorated with the golden earrings of the Midianites and the ornaments and chains from their camels; it was a kind of "war monument." But whatever intentions Gideon may have had for it, it became an object of worship: ["all Israel went thither a whoring after it"]{.class-0} (vs. 27). The reference is to spiritual adultery (Hos. 4:12--14; James 4:4).

That is why Jehovah's Witnesses won't salute a flag: they consider it an act of worship to an idol. Of course, you aren't worshiping a flag when you salute it. The flag represents something. In our daily vacation Bible school, the kids pledge allegiance to the American flag, to the Christian flag, and to the Bible. The American flag represents the country, and the Christian flag represents Jesus Christ. They are promising to be loyal Amerians and loyal followers of the Lord Jesus Christ. There's nothing wrong with that.

Now we don't have any flags on the platform of the Bible Baptist Church. It's not that I have any convictions against flags; they're just a secular touch for which I don't care. But I must admit, the longer I live, the less patriotic I have become. It's hard to have a patriotic spirit with someone like Obama pushing sex perversion, Socialism, and Mohammedanism on the American public. But I wouldn't say anything if a man like Evangelist Tim Lee held a "God and Country" rally with the flag, the military color guard, and "The Star Spangled Banner" being played and sung. The fellow gave his legs in the service of his country; I figure he has the right to be patriotic.

[But if you put your country before your Saviour, your country becomes an idol. Israel had a bad habit of taking good things and turning them into idols. They took the brazen serpent God told Moses to raise in the wilderness and burned incense to it; Hezekiah had to brake it in ]{.class-22}[pieces and call it ]{.class-33}["Nehushtan"]{.class-34}[---"just a piece of brass" ]{.class-33}[(2 Kings 18:4).]{.class-22}

Whether or not Gideon meant for his gold-studded ephod to become an idol or not, that is what it quickly became. The text says, the ["thing became a snare unto Gideon, and to his house."]{.class-0} Gideon introduced the idolatry into which Israel would fall after his death (vs. 33), and just like David's family had to suffer the consequences of his adultery with Bathsheba, Gideon's household is going to suffer the consequences of his "war monument."

28 Thus was Midian subdued before the children of Israel, so that they lifted up their heads no more. And the country was in quietness forty years in the days of Gideon.

29 And Jerubbaal the son of Joash went and dwelt in his own house.

30 And Gideon had threescore and ten sons of his body begotten: for he had many wives.

31 And his concubine that was in Shechem, she also bare him a son, whose name he called Abimelech.

The passage gives the aftermath of the defeat of Midian. Because Gideon pursued the Midianite host until he had completely destroyed it and took its Kings, Midian was never again able to invade the land. The time of quiet following was forty years (vs. 28).

Notice again the phenomenon of seven and forty, which we discussed in 6:1 (which see). ["Seven"]{.class-0} was back in verse 26, and ["forty"]{.class-0} shows up here in verse 28.

In verse 19, Gideon returns to his home, and that is where the trouble starts. Not only does he set up the ephod ["in his city"]{.class-0} (vs. 27), he marries ["many wives."]{.class-0} Although Gideon refused to be King, he committed two sins absolutely forbidden to the Kings of Israel. In Deuteronomy 17:17, the Kings were forbidden to ["multiply wives"]{.class-0} or ["silver and gold."]{.class-0} Of course, Gideon wasn't a King, but as the judge of Israel at this time, he would have done well to take heed to the verse anyway. He collected 1,700 shekels of gold earrings and ended up making an idol out of them. He had ["many wives,"]{.class-0} as well as a ["concubine"]{.class-0} (vs. 31), and because of that, a lot of people die, as we shall see in the next chapter.

32 And Gideon the son of Joash died in a good old age, and was buried in the sepulchre of Joash his father, in Ophrah of the Abiezrites.

33 And it came to pass, as soon as Gideon was dead, that the children of Israel turned again, and went a whoring after Baalim, and made Baalberith their god.

34 And the children of Israel remembered not the LORD their God, who had delivered them out of the hands of all their enemies on every side:

35 Neither shewed they kindness to the house of Jerubbaal, namely, Gideon, according to all the goodness which he had shewed unto Israel.

Gideon lived to ["a good old age"]{.class-0} and died. He was buried in his daddy's sepulcher in his hometown of ["Ophrah of the Abiezrites"]{.class-0} (vs. 32). And true to the pattern found in the book of Judges (see 2:18--19), once Gideon was dead, Israel fell back into full blown idolatry.

We have discussed ["Baalim"]{.class-0} (vs. 33) under 2:13 (which see). Baalim were the "little Baals" that came from the sexual union of ["Baal and Ashtaroth"]{.class-0} (2:13). This time, the "Baal" that was elevated to the point of supremacy was ["Baal-berith"]{.class-0}; that is, the idol of Baal located in the city of Berith. You see the same thing with the Roman Catholics today: "Our Lady of Guadalupe," "Our Lady of Fatima," "Our Lady of Lourdes," etc. Those are all idols of Mary located in those cities. Rabbinical tradition, for whatever it's worth, identifies Baal-berith as the same god as Baalzebub (see 2 Kings 1:2). The name Baal-berith means "lord of the covenant" and could imply that the children of Israel entered into a covenant with the heathen (see Exod 34:12) that was sealed by worshiping their god. Another explanation is since the idol is connected with Shechem in the next chapter, the "covenant" could be a reference to Shechem. It was Shechem where Joshua made a covenant with the people before he died (Josh. 24:25).

["And the children of Israel remembered not the LORD their God . . . Neither shewed they kindness to the house of Jerubbaal, namely, Gideon"]{.class-0} (vss. 34--35). Those Israelites were ungrateful. They didn't remember God delivering them, and they didn't remember Gideon risking his life for them.

Many years ago, when I was stationed in Japan as music officer for Radio Tokyo, I did a favor for a fellow named Arima-San; he was the director of the Tokyo Philharmonic Orchestra. He was in a rough situation, and I "went to bats" for him. I wasn't able to get him what he wanted, but I stuck out my neck for him and had a pretty stiff go around with a Colonel over it.

Do you know what Arima-San did in response for me sticking up for him? For five years after I returned to the States, I got a Christmas card from him that read, "Never forget your kindness." He did that for an American whom he never saw again. "Never forget your kindness." Why is it a lost Japanese can do that for a depraved sinner like I was, and yet God's people forget His goodness to them. That Bible says, ["Bless the LORD, O my soul, and forget not all his benefits"]{.class-0} (Psa. 103:2).

Second Chronicles 24:22 says, ["Thus Joash the king remembered not the kindness which Jehoiada his father had done to him, but slew his son."]{.class-0} Can you imagine that? But it happened. It would be like that Jew in Luke 10 who was rescued by the Good Samaritan getting better and killing his benefactor.

That is a picture of a Christian whom the Lord saved out of a sure enough mess and cleaned him up, and that Christian pays the Lord back by going into worse sin that he committed before he trusted Christ.

You say, "A real Christian wouldn't do that, Ruckman."

Sure he would; haven't you ever read 1 Corinthians 5:1--5? There was a saved man who was committing a sin so heinous that the lost Gentiles in Corinth wouldn't even think of committing it (1 Cor. 5:1). Do you know where he was headed? [Heaven]{.class-1} (1 Cor. 5:5)!

Brethren, all you have to do is put down that Bible, stop praying, drop out of church, and you can end up committing any sin a lost person can commit---and sometimes [worse.]{.class-1} One solution is to ["forget not all his benefits." "The goodness of God"]{.class-0} should lead you ["to repentance,"]{.class-0} and when you forget that goodness, you end up despising God's goodness, forbearance, and longsuffering (Rom. 2:4) and living like hell.

[]{#section-0_1_2d6f393ea0e2de31_7093.xhtml}

Chapter 9

9:1 And Abimelech the son of Jerubbaal went to Shechem unto his mother's brethren, and communed with them, and with all the family of the house of his mother's father, saying,

2 Speak, I pray you, in the ears of all the men of Shechem, Whether is better for you, either that all the sons of Jerubbaal, which are threescore and ten persons, reign over you, or that one reign over you? remember also that I am your bone and your flesh.

3 And his mother's brethren spake of him in the ears of all the men of Shechem all these words: and their hearts inclined to follow Abimelech; for they said, He is our brother.

4 And they gave him threescore and ten pieces of silver out of the house of Baalberith, wherewith Abimelech hired vain and light persons, which followed him.

5 And he went unto his father's house at Ophrah, and slew his brethren the sons of Jerubbaal, being threescore and ten persons, upon one stone: notwithstanding yet Jotham the youngest son of Jerubbaal was left; for he hid himself.

6 And all the men of Shechem gathered together, and all the house of Millo, and went, and made Abimelech king, by the plain of the pillar that was in Shechem.

This is Abimelech, the son of Gideon who was set apart from Gideon's other seventy sons back in 8:30--31 because he was the son of a concubine and not a full wife. Notice throughout the chapter that it's not the name Gideon that is used, but the name given to Gideon by his father when Gideon broke down the image of Baal in the grove (6:30--32): Jerubbaal. The reason for that is to contrast Abimelech, who followed Baal-berith (vs. 4), with his father who contended with Baal (Jerubbaal---6:32).

The infidels trying to find fault with the Bible attempt to create a contradiction between Judges 9:1 and 2 Samuel 11:21. Was Abimelech ["the son of Jerubbaal"]{.class-0} (Judg. 9:1) or ["the son of Jerubbesheth"]{.class-0} (2 Sam. 11:21)? Of course, there is no contradiction. David, following the admonition of Joshua in Joshua 23:7, is not mentioning the name of a false god. Jerubbaal means "contender with Baal"; Jerubbesheth means "contender with the shame." "The shame" is obviously the idol of Baal.

Now while Gideon refused to be King, Abimelech is perfectly willing to take the office on himself. He is from Shechem. Shechem was a city of refuge in Ephraim's inheritance (Josh. 20:7). It is where Joshua made the covenant for Israel to follow God after he died (Josh. 24:25), and where Joseph's bones were buried (Josh. 24:32). Shechem is where Rehoboam went to be crowned King after his father Solomon's death (1 Kings 12:1). Shechem was also the first city Jeroboam fortified against Rehoboam and Judah (1 Kings 12:25).

Knowing that his seventy brothers won't give him any kind of hearing when it comes to making him the King, he goes to ["the family of the house of his mother's father"]{.class-0} (vs. 1). He's going to "suck up" to his momma's kin and get them to side with him against his seventy brothers.

Notice Abimelech's question to the men of Shechem: ["Whether is BETTER FOR YOU . . . ?"]{.class-0} (vs. 2); [not,]{.class-1} "What is RIGHT?" It is a matter of [expediency]{.class-1} for the men of Shechem, not a matter of what [God said.]{.class-1}

["Whether is better for you, either that all the sons of Jerubbaal . . . reign over you, or that one reign over you? REMEMBER ALSO THAT I AM YOUR BONE AND YOUR FLESH."]{.class-0} In other words, "If you put me into office, I'll give you favors Gideon's other sons won't because we're kinfolk." That's how a lot of small country Southern Baptist churches work. Everyone in the church is related to everyone else, and they'll tell the pastor what to do or give him the boot if he tries crossing them.

The flattery works. The men of Shechem go to the temple of Baal-berith and take seventy ["pieces of silver"]{.class-0} out of the treasury and give the money to Abimelech (vs. 4). With the money, Abimelech hires ["vain and light persons"]{.class-0} to follow him. ["Vain"]{.class-0}---the men are [worthless; ]{.class-1}they're [good for nothing.]{.class-1} ["Light"]{.class-0}---we would say "shallow"; they have no real convictions about anything and are just in it for the money. What they turn out to be are [thugs:]{.class-1} they help Abimelech kill his brothers (vs. 5).

But despite Abimelech's best efforts, Gideon's youngest son, Jotham, escapes. In the meantime, ["the men of Shechem"]{.class-0} go to where Joshua made the covenant with Israel (Josh. 24:26--27). The stone Joshua set up as a witness was ["the pillar that was in Shechem"]{.class-0} (vs. 6).

7 And when they told it to Jotham, he went and stood in the top of mount Gerizim, and lifted up his voice, and cried, and said unto them, Hearken unto me, ye men of Shechem, that God may hearken unto you.

8 The trees went forth on a time to anoint a king over them; and they said unto the olive tree, Reign thou over us.

9 But the olive tree said unto them, Should I leave my fatness, wherewith by me they honour God and man, and go to be promoted over the trees?

10 And the trees said to the fig tree, Come thou, and reign over us.

11 But the fig tree said unto them, Should I forsake my sweetness, and my good fruit, and go to be promoted over the trees?

12 Then said the trees unto the vine, Come thou, and reign over us.

13 And the vine said unto them, Should I leave my wine, which cheereth God and man, and go to be promoted over the trees?

14 Then said all the trees unto the bramble, Come thou, and reign over us.

15 And the bramble said unto the trees, If in truth ye anoint me king over you, then come and put your trust in my shadow: and if not, let fire come out of the bramble, and devour the cedars of Lebanon.

I have separated this parable about the trees from Jotham's application in verses 16--20 because there is more here than a parable against Abimelech. What follows is a description of the trees in the Garden of Eden (see more in Ezek. 31:8). Without even knowing what he is saying (cf. 1 Pet. 1:11), Jotham is about to give you the identity of the Tree of Life and the Tree of the Knowledge of Good and Evil.

["Mount Gerizim"]{.class-0} (vs. 7) is where the blessings of the Law were read when the Israelites entered the land of Canaan (Deut. 11:29; Josh. 8:33--34). Shechem was just north and east of Mt. Gerizim. Jotham is at the top of the mountain yelling over into Shechem.

["Hearken unto me, ye men of Shechem, that God may hearken unto you."]{.class-0} The word ["hearken,"]{.class-0} here, means more than just "listen"; it means "pay attention to." Jotham is telling the Shechemites that they had better pay attention to what he is saying because God is about to show some special attention to them for what was done to Gideon's sons. If they would have paid attention to Jotham's parable, they would have repented and turned Abimelech over to the justice of God and thrown themselves on the mercy of God for forgiveness. The attention that God is going to give them, they don't want.

Now there are three trees named in the parable, along with ["the bramble"]{.class-0} (vss. 14--15). The three trees are: 1) ["the olive tree"]{.class-0} (vss. 8--9), 2) ["the fig tree"]{.class-0} (vss. 10--11), and 3) ["the vine"]{.class-0} (vss. 12--13). ["The fig tree"]{.class-0} and ["the bramble"]{.class-0} give you the connection to the Garden of Eden (Gen. 3:7, 18). The fig tree represents [self-righteousness.]{.class-1} It was the tree from which Adam and Eve got leaves to cover up their nakedness (Gen. 3:7). In the Parable of the Fig Tree in Luke 13:6--9, the owner of the vineyard wanted to cut the fig tree down because it didn't bear fruit. That is why Christ cursed the fig tree in Mark 11:12--14. It had leaves with no fruit. The idea was that the leaves were a [profession]{.class-1} of fruit without the [possession ]{.class-1}of[ ]{.class-1}fruit.

The brambles weren't actually in the Garden of Eden, but the Lord talked about them there (Gen. 3:18). In the parable, it represents the wrong King, so it is a picture of God punishing the earth by letting Satan's christ reign for three-and-a-half years in the Tribulation (Rev. 13:5).

That leaves two named trees in the Garden of Eden (the Tree of Life and the Tree of Knowledge of Good and Evil) and two named trees here in the parable (the olive and the vine). So let's take these two trees and see how they match up to the two trees in the Garden. First, let's take the olive tree.

Here in verse 9, the olive is made to ["honour God and man."]{.class-0} Olive oil was used to anoint the priests and the Kings of Israel. Olive oil was used to light the Golden Candlestick in the Tabernacle (Exod. 27:20), which was a picture of the Holy Spirit (Rev. 4:5). Olive trees also represent the two witnesses in the Tribulation (Zech. 4:3, 11--12; Rev. 11:4). In Romans 11:17--24, the olive tree represents the opportunity to hear the Gospel and be saved. In Genesis 8:11, when God wanted to show Noah that life was returning to the earth, He had the dove (another picture of the Holy Spirit---Matt. 3:16) bring back an olive leaf plucked off a live, growing tree. Given all the symbolism in the Scriptures, without a doubt the Tree of Life was an olive tree---a supernatural olive tree, I'll grant you, but an olive tree nevertheless.

The fruit of the Tree of the Knowledge of Good and Evil was forbidden to Adam and Eve. Well, the one thing forbidden throughout Scripture from being eaten or drunk is [blood]{.class-1}. It was forbidden [before the Law]{.class-1} (Gen. 9:4), [under the Law]{.class-1} (Lev. 17:14), and [after the Law]{.class-1} (Acts 15:20). Well, it is the fruit of the vine tree that is likened to blood (see our comments on John 2:3--4 and Matt. 26:27--29 in those Commentaries). It is the only tree forbidden elsewhere in Scripture (Num. 6:3--4). So the forbidden fruit eaten by Adam and Eve in the Garden of Eden wasn't an apple or an apricot; it was [supernatural grapes.]{.class-1}

Now, in the parable, the trees want a King, and they come to the olive tree, the fig tree, and the vine tree, and ask for each of them in turn to become King. Each of them turns down the offer with the same basic answer: "Why should I want to become King? It would be a [step down]{.class-1} from my present state."

The angel told Nebuchadnezzar that when it came to the Gentile Kingdoms of this world, the Lord sets over them ["the basest ]{.class-0}\[i.e., the [lowest]{.class-1}\] [of men"]{.class-0} (Dan. 4:17). So when it came time for these trees to get a King, they get one that is [low to the ground.]{.class-1} The bramble isn't even a tree; it's a bush growing only about two or three feet above ground. Can you imagine a tree five foot tall, ten foot tall, or twenty foot tall trying to get under the shadow of a bramble bush (vs. 15)?

The bramble bush accepts the offer to be King over the trees; but if at any time they don't trust it to make the right decisions, to give the right orders, and to lead them right, the bramble will send out fire to burn them up. Note ["the cedars of Lebanon"]{.class-0} in verse 15. These, along with the firs and chestnuts, are the other trees identified in the Garden of Eden (see Ezek. 31:8). In Ezekiel 31:8, it is said the cedars in the Garden couldn't hide the tree that represented the Antichrist. So in the application, the bramble bush represents Abimelech, and he is one of eighteen types of the Antichrist in Scripture (see chap. 3 in our work [The Mark of the Beast]{.class-1}).
