16 Now therefore, if ye have done truly and sincerely, in that ye have made Abimelech king, and if ye have dealt well with Jerubbaal and his house, and have done unto him according to the deserving of his hands;

17 (For my father fought for you, and adventured his life far, and delivered you out of the hand of Midian:

18 And ye are risen up against my father's house this day, and have slain his sons, threescore and ten persons, upon one stone, and have made Abimelech, the son of his maidservant, king over the men of Shechem, because he is your brother;)

19 If ye then have dealt truly and sincerely with Jerubbaal and with his house this day, then rejoice ye in Abimelech, and let him also rejoice in you:

20 But if not, let fire come out from Abimelech, and devour the men of Shechem, and the house of Millo; and let fire come out from the men of Shechem, and from the house of Millo, and devour Abimelech.

21 And Jotham ran away, and fled, and went to Beer, and dwelt there, for fear of Abimelech his brother.

The passage is Jotham making application of his parable. The bramble bush, as we have seen, is Abimelech. ["The cedars of Lebanon"]{.class-0} are the men of Shechem who made Abimelech King. The olive, fig, and vine trees turn out to be types of the other seventy sons of Gideon who, like their father, refused to be King.

Jotham's message is this: "If you have done right (["truly and sincerely"]{.class-0}) by killing Gideon's sons after he fought against the Midianites for you, and by making his illegitimate son King because he's kin to you, then don't worry about a thing. Relax; be happy with each other. But if you did the wrong thing, then bang your heads against each other until you've consumed each other."

The ["fire"]{.class-0} that comes ["out from Abimelech"]{.class-0} (vs. 20) turns out to be literal (vs. 49). Prophetically, it points to the ten Kings under the Antichrist burning the Great Whore with fire (Rev. 17:16).

Jotham, having spoken his piece, flees in fear so Abimelech won't finish what he started in verse 5. Notice that Jotham ["went to Beer"]{.class-0} when he got scared. Of course that Beer wasn't Busch, Miller, Pabst Blue Ribbon, Schlitz, Budweiser, Heineken, or Sam Adams. Beer is an abbreviation that could apply to any one of six cities in Israel. The name first appears in Numbers 21:16. If you were to go down to your local bar and ask the first ten drunks who come out what the word [beer]{.class-1} means, they wouldn't have the faintest idea at all. It has nothing at all to do with fermented hops; it means  "a well of water."

22 When Abimelech had reigned three years over Israel,

23 Then God sent an evil spirit between Abimelech and the men of Shechem; and the men of Shechem dealt treacherously with Abimelech:

24 That the cruelty done to the threescore and ten sons of Jerubbaal might come, and their blood be laid upon Abimelech their brother, which slew them; and upon the men of Shechem, which aided him in the killing of his brethren.

25 And the men of Shechem set liers in wait for him in the top of the mountains, and they robbed all that came along that way by them: and it was told Abimelech.

Three years go by with no sign of any dispute or disagreement between Abimelech and the Shechemites. I am sure they must have thought they had done the right thing since things were going just fine. But the Bible says, ["Because sentence against an evil work is not executed speedily, therefore the heart of the sons of men is fully set in them to do evil"]{.class-0} (Eccl. 8:11). God had not forgotten what had been done to Gideon's sons.

That should be a lesson to the United States. Just because catastrophic judgment hasn't fallen on this nation [yet]{.class-1} doesn't mean God is overlooking this country's drunkenness, pornography, occultism, self-righteousness, false religions, murders, thefts, corrupting of our children, drug use, Bible rejection, sex perversion, etc. In God's good time, the axe is going to fall, and when it does, it is going to fall hard.

Verse 23: ["Then God sent an evil spirit between Abimelech and the men of Shechem."]{.class-0} [God]{.class-1} sent the evil spirit, not the Devil. That is what happened to Saul: ["an evil spirit FROM THE LORD troubled him"]{.class-0} (1 Sam. 16:14). If you want to see how it is done, read 1 Kings 22:19--23.

["The men of Shechem,"]{.class-0} Abimelech's former buddies, got together and made a conspiracy against him. As long as they had a common enemy (Gideon's other sons), they got along just fine, but once the common ground was gone, eventually they began to fight amongst themselves. You see something like that in the New Testament. Left to themselves, Pilate and Herod were enemies, but when called upon to put Jesus Christ to death, they became buddies (Luke 23:12). For further comment, see our remarks under Joshua 9:2 in that Commentary.

A good cross-reference to verse 24 is Psalm 7:16.

"His mischief shall return upon his own head, and his violent dealing shall come down upon his own pate."

Psalm 7:16 is a prophecy about the end of the Antichrist at the return of Jesus Christ in power. It is a fulfillment of the first prophecy in the Bible---Genesis 3:15. In that verse, the Seed of the woman bruises the head of the seed of the serpent. According to Romans 16:20, that DID NOT take place at Calvary; it awaits future fulfillment.

The reason Psalm 7:16 applies to Judges 9:24 is that Abimelech is a type of the Antichrist. As a type of the Antichrist, for his cruelty to his brothers, Abimelech gets a deadly head wound that would have killed him if his armorbearer hadn't finished him off first (vss. 53--54).

In verse 25, the Shechemites plot against Abimelech and set robbers along the mountain passes to cut him off from the resources he needed to keep the region under his thumb. Of course it doesn't take long for Abimelech to learn of their betrayal.

26 And Gaal the son of Ebed came with his brethren, and went over to Shechem: and the men of Shechem put their confidence in him.

27 And they went out into the fields, and gathered their vineyards, and trode the grapes, and made merry, and went into the house of their god, and did eat and drink, and cursed Abimelech.

28 And Gaal the son of Ebed said, Who is Abimelech, and who is Shechem, that we should serve him? is not he the son of Jerubbaal? and Zebul his officer? serve the men of Hamor the father of Shechem: for why should we serve him?

29 And would to God this people were under my hand! then would I remove Abimelech. And he said to Abimelech, Increase thine army, and come out.

Just as ["the men of Shechem"]{.class-0} chose Abimelech as their leader, they now dump him and get ["Gaal the son of Ebed"]{.class-0} in his place. To celebrate their new leader, they decide to hold a drunken orgy (vs. 27). Instead of eating, drinking, and being merry (Eccl. 8:15; Luke 12:19), it's eat, drink, and [cuss.]{.class-1} A lot of that goes on nowadays.

["Who is Abimelech, and who is Shechem, that we should serve him? . . . serve the men of Hamor the father of Shechem: for why should we serve him?"]{.class-0} (vs. 28). In other words, "Abimelech is no better than the brothers he slew; if we had to choose between Abimelech and the original inhabitants of Shechem (["Hamor the father of Shechem"]{.class-0}---Gen. 34), we would be better off with the original Hivites who founded the city." Gaal is stirring up the city against Abimelech.

["Would to God this people were under my hand!"]{.class-0} (vs. 29). That's what Absalom said in 2 Samuel 15:4---["Oh that I were made judge in the land."]{.class-0}

["Then would I remove Abimelech. And he said to Abimelech, Increase thine army, and come out."]{.class-0} That's easy to say when you're drinking yourself senseless. It's quite another thing to say when you're face to face with the enemy on the battlefield. One time the King of Syria sent word to Ahab that he was going to come down and whip Ahab and his army, and the dust of Israel wouldn't even suffice in number when compared with the Syrian army (1 Kings 20:10).

Now Ahab may have been a wicked King, but he said something very profound in response to Benhadad's "trash talk." Ahab said, ["Let not him that girdeth on his harness boast himself as he that putteth it off"]{.class-0} (1 Kings 20:11). In other words, "Don't count your chickens before they're hatched, Benhadad. All kinds of things can happen in a battle---fortunes of war and all that. You haven't won yet." Good advice. Gaal is bragging about how he can whip Abimelech no matter what size army he has, and he's never been up against Abimelech one time to test his mettle.

30 And when Zebul the ruler of the city heard the words of Gaal the son of Ebed, his anger was kindled.

31 And he sent messengers unto Abimelech privily, saying, Behold, Gaal the son of Ebed and his brethren be come to Shechem; and, behold, they fortify the city against thee.

32 Now therefore up by night, thou and the people that is with thee, and lie in wait in the field:

33 And it shall be, that in the morning, as soon as the sun is up, thou shalt rise early, and set upon the city: and, behold, when he and the people that is with him come out against thee, then mayest thou do to them as thou shalt find occasion.

34 And Abimelech rose up, and all the people that were with him, by night, and they laid wait against Shechem in four companies.

35 And Gaal the son of Ebed went out, and stood in the entering of the gate of the city: and Abimelech rose up, and the people that were with him, from lying in wait.

36 And when Gaal saw the people, he said to Zebul, Behold, there come people down from the top of the mountains. And Zebul said unto him, Thou seest the shadow of the mountains as if they were men.

37 And Gaal spake again and said, See there come people down by the middle of the land, and another company come along by the plain of Meonenim.

38 Then said Zebul unto him, Where is now thy mouth, wherewith thou saidst, Who is Abimelech, that we should serve him? is not this the people that thou hast despised? go out, I pray now, and fight with them.

39 And Gaal went out before the men of Shechem, and fought with Abimelech.

40 And Abimelech chased him, and he fled before him, and many were overthrown and wounded, even unto the entering of the gate.

["Zebul"]{.class-0} (vs. 30) is the ["officer"]{.class-0} (vs. 28) Abimelech left in charge of Shechem when he was away. As it turned out, after convincing the citizens of Shechem to make him King because he was their kin, he didn't even live at Shechem. Verse 41 says he ["dwelt at Arumah."]{.class-0}

Still being loyal to Abimelech, and not being able to stomach Gaal's mouth (vs. 38), Zebul sends word to Abimelech secretly (["privily,"]{.class-0} vs. 31) about what Gaal was doing. He asked Abimelech to get his men together and travel by night, and be ready to attack Gaal in the morning.

Unlike his father who followed God's battle plan, Abimelech set his ambush ["in four companies"]{.class-0} (vs. 34). This became the T.O. for a rifle regiment when I was in the service. The battalion had four companies in it: three rifle companies and a heavy weapons company. With this set-up, three companies are used in action (see vs. 43).

When Gaal wakes up in the morning, he goes out to the gates of the city. At that time of day, the city lay in the shadows of the surrounding mountains. Gaal swore he saw men coming down the sides of the mountains, but Zebul said, "Nah, you're just seeing the shadows from the mountains." "No," said Gaal, "there's men there and over there."

By now Zebul no longer bothered trying to fool Gaal anymore. "Where's your big mouth now, bub," he said. "These are the people you said you could whip, so go out and whip them."

But Gaal wasn't up to the task. Abimelech set Gaal and his men fleeing back to the city, and a lot of them died and were wounded at the gate trying to get back into the city.

41 And Abimelech dwelt at Arumah: and Zebul thrust out Gaal and his brethren, that they should not dwell in Shechem.

42 And it came to pass on the morrow, that the people went out into the field; and they told Abimelech.

43 And he took the people, and divided them into three companies, and laid wait in the field, and looked, and, behold, the people were come forth out of the city; and he rose up against them, and smote them.

44 And Abimelech, and the company that was with him, rushed forward, and stood in the entering of the gate of the city: and the two other companies ran upon all the people that were in the fields, and slew them.

45 And Abimelech fought against the city all that day; and he took the city, and slew the people that was therein, and beat down the city, and sowed it with salt.

Gaal makes it back into the city with his army decimated. While Abimelech has moved his men back overnight to regroup for another attack on the city, Zebul takes Gaal and what's left of his men and throws them out of the city. The idea is if Abimelech sees his enemy has been ejected from the city, he'll leave Shechem alone.

But the Shechemites did not take into account Abimelech's cruelty. This is the fellow who slaughtered his own brothers, even though they had done him no wrong. He is not just after Gaal, he is now seeking vengeance on the inhabitants of Shechem who robbed him in the mountain passes and let Gaal in to overthrow him.

After Abimelech finishes with Gaal, he fights against the city. He kills the people, breaks down the walls, and sows the fields with salt so none of his survivors can grow crops to feed themselves.

46 And when all the men of the tower of Shechem heard that, they entered into an hold of the house of the god Berith.

47 And it was told Abimelech, that all the men of the tower of Shechem were gathered together.

48 And Abimelech gat him up to mount Zalmon, he and all the people that were with him; and Abimelech took an axe in his hand, and cut down a bough from the trees, and took it, and laid it on his shoulder, and said unto the people that were with him, What ye have seen me do, make haste, and do as I have done.

49 And all the people likewise cut down every man his bough, and followed Abimelech, and put them to the hold, and set the hold on fire upon them; so that all the men of the tower of Shechem died also, about a thousand men and women.

The remaining men and women in the city (around a thousand in number, vs. 49) flee to a stronghold in the temple of Baal-berith. This was a tower in the temple complex.

In fulfillment of Jotham's curse in verse 20, Abimelech goes to a mountain near the city of Shechem. There he goes to the forest and cuts a bough off one of the trees. He says to his men, ["What ye have seen me do, make haste, and do as I have done"]{.class-0} (vs. 48). Leading by example. We have already commented on this under 7:17 (which see).

Each of Abimelech's men cuts a bough off a tree and follows him back to the tower. There they surround the base of the tower with the boughs and set them on fire, burning the tower down and everyone in it.

The ironic thing, here, is that the tower of Shechem was in the temple of Baal-berith. The people of Shechem had funded Abimelech's vendetta against his brothers from the treasury of Baal-berith (vs. 4). Now they are paying for that sin in the place where it all started.

["Be not deceived; God is not mocked: for whatsoever a man soweth, that shall he also reap"]{.class-0} (Gal. 6:7).

50 Then went Abimelech to Thebez, and encamped against Thebez, and took it.

51 But there was a strong tower within the city, and thither fled all the men and women, and all they of the city, and shut it to them, and gat them up to the top of the tower.

52 And Abimelech came unto the tower, and fought against it, and went hard unto the door of the tower to burn it with fire.

53 And a certain woman cast a piece of a millstone upon Abimelech's head, and all to brake his skull.

54 Then he called hastily unto the young man his armourbearer, and said unto him, Draw thy sword, and slay me, that men say not of me, A woman slew him. And his young man thrust him through, and he died.

55 And when the men of Israel saw that Abimelech was dead, they departed every man unto his place.

56 Thus God rendered the wickedness of Abimelech, which he did unto his father, in slaying his seventy brethren:

57 And all the evil of the men of Shechem did God render upon their heads: and upon them came the curse of Jotham the son of Jerubbaal.

Abimelech goes on to Thebez to set an example to any other city that might rebel against him. The same thing happens in Thebez as happened in Shechem: Abimelech takes the city; the people flee to their tower; Abimelech goes about to burn down the tower. But this time, God turns the tables on Abimelech. Instead of the people inside the tower just sitting back until they are "crispy critters," as Abimelech goes up to set the boughs on fire, a woman in the tower throws out a piece of millstone with the intent of hitting Abimelech on the head and braking his skull. The smallest millstone weighed no less than fifteen pounds, and this was just a piece of a much larger one. A 25--35 pound rock would crack your skull like an egg shell.

The woman hits her target, and Abimelech lays on the ground dying. But in Oriental culture, it is a great shame for an able-bodied man to be killed by a woman or child. Abimelech wants to die with honor, so he asks his armorbearer to thrust him through with his sword so no one can say, ["A woman slew him"]{.class-0} (vs. 54). The armorbearer obliges him. Of course it does Abimelech no good. Later on when David refers to this incident in 2 Samuel 11:21, he attributes the death of Abimelech to the woman. You aren't going to get by ["the Judge of all the earth"]{.class-0} (Gen. 18:25) on a technicality.

So God paid both Abimelech and the people in Shechem back for their wickedness against the house of Gideon. With their false King dead, all the Israelites who followed him went home (vs. 55 cf. 1 Kings 22:35--36).

Judges 9 is a parenthesis in the narrative of the judges. Abimelech wasn't a judge because he wasn't chosen by God, he didn't deliver Israel from any heathen nation, and he didn't lead Israel in getting right with God. He was not counted as the first King of Israel because he was not anointed by God to the position and he only ruled over a small part of central Israel. He operated completely without reference to God or anything He said.

Sadly, that can be said of a lot of Christians in this day and time. Some Christians operate on nothing but selfish ambition, just like Abimelech. This is even true in the local church. The Apostle John said these kind of people just want the preeminent place that belongs to Jesus Christ (3 John 9 cf. Col. 1:18). The Lord said He hates the doctrine of these people (the clergy ruling over the laity---Rev. 2:15) and said He would ["fight against them with the sword of his mouth"]{.class-0} (Rev. 2:16). The church that is guilty of this as an organization is, of course, the Roman Catholic Church, but God isn't any more pleased with a Baptist who does it than He is with a Catholic (see our comments on 1 Pet. 5:1--3 in that Commentary). God hates what I call "church operators" no matter what their denomination. They will make a wreck of a local church just like Abimelech made a wreck of Israel.

[]{#section-0_1_cf861093681f5899_7094.xhtml}

Chapter 10

10:1 And after Abimelech there arose to defend Israel Tola the son of Puah, the son of Dodo, a man of Issachar; and he dwelt in Shamir in mount Ephraim.

2 And he judged Israel twenty and three years, and died, and was buried in Shamir.

3 And after him arose Jair, a Gileadite, and judged Israel twenty and two years.

4 And he had thirty sons that rode on thirty ass colts, and they had thirty cities, which are called Havothjair unto this day, which are in the land of Gilead.

5 And Jair died, and was buried in Camon.

["And after Abimelech there arose to defend Israel Tola"]{.class-0} (vs. 1). Now we are back on track. After the fiasco with Abimelech, God raises up the seventh judge: ["Tola the son of Puah."]{.class-0} He ["arose to defend Israel."]{.class-0} This time, though, there is no mention of any major attack by a heathen nation. The implication is that Tola armed the people and kept them in readiness for an invasion so nobody would attack them.

The next judge, number eight, is ["Jair, a Gileadite"]{.class-0} (vs. 3). This is the first judge from the east side of Jordan. He is named after a famous grandson of Manasseh who took some small towns in Gilead and Bashan that he named after himself (Num. 32:41; Deut. 3:14). His namesake here does the same thing with the cities his sons judge.

As we have already discussed in our comments on 5:10 (which see), asses were the "Cadillac," the "limousine," for rulers in Bible times. Jair's sons served as judges under him, each boy ruling over a particular city in Gilead. And just as we told you back in 5:10, these ["ass colts"]{.class-0} point prophetically to the Jewish saints who return with Jesus Christ at the Second Advent to rule and reign with Him in His kingdom.

Between Tola and Jair, the land has 45 years of peace, but after Jair dies, Israel falls once more into apostasy.

6 And the children of Israel did evil again in the sight of the LORD, and served Baalim, and Ashtaroth, and the gods of Syria, and the gods of Zidon, and the gods of Moab, and the gods of the children of Ammon, and the gods of the Philistines, and forsook the LORD, and served not him.

7 And the anger of the LORD was hot against Israel, and he sold them into the hands of the Philistines, and into the hands of the children of Ammon.

8 And that year they vexed and oppressed the children of Israel: eighteen years, all the children of Israel that were on the other side Jordan in the land of the Amorites, which is in Gilead.

9 Moreover the children of Ammon passed over Jordan to fight also against Judah, and against Benjamin, and against the house of Ephraim; so that Israel was sore distressed.

Notice the nations mentioned in verse 6. Of the five nations named, four are connected with descendants of [Ham.]{.class-1} The Zidonians came from Canaan (Gen. 10:6, 15). The Moabites and Ammonites were from Lot's two half-breed daughters, whose mother was an African from Egypt (Gen. 12:10, 13:1, 19:37--38). The Philistines were from Mizraim (Gen. 10:13--14). This modern movement to make "God" a black, Lesbian woman is a return to the [jungle.]{.class-1}

While ["the gods of Syria"]{.class-0} are nowhere given by name in the Scriptures, the gods of Zidon, Moab, Ammon, and Philistia are. Zidon was the seat of Baal and Ashtoreth worship (1 Kings 11:33, 16:31). The Moabites worshiped Chemosh, and the Ammonites worshiped Milcom (1 Kings 11:33). The Philistine gods included Dagon (Judg. 16:23; 1 Sam. 5:2) and Baalzebub (2 Kings 1:2--3). Of course, historically, these were polytheistic societies which had a plethora of gods, but those are the ones named in Scripture. For further study, get Alexander Hislop's work [The Two Babylons.]{.class-1}

When it comes to gods, modern man is no different. Unsaved men are always shopping for gods. The Bible says, ["there is none that seeketh after God"]{.class-0} (Rom. 3:11). Men may seek for a god, but they are not seeking after the God of the Book. They are looking for a god to fit their "lifestyle." Voltaire, an unsaved infidel, made an astute observation once: "God created man in His own image; then man returned the favor." But on occasion God will honor the efforts of an unsaved man to find Him, even if that search is misdirected.

Before I was saved, I professed to be in a quest, of sorts, for God. I had read all the religious literature---[outside of the Bible]{.class-1}---and I headed over to Japan to learn Buddhism. God looked at that mess and said, "I'll see just how much he wants to find me." So He let me go to the end of my rope, and then He put a Bible in my hands. Before that, I had thrown one in the trash and called another one pig's meat. When the Lord finally told me to get a Bible (see the details in [The Full Cup]{.class-1}), I had to break into a flophouse and steal one. But I read that Bible until my nerves were frayed. Everywhere I turned it was judgment, doom, and pestilence. I couldn't find the Gospel. So God sent Hugh Pyle to me. He showed me the Gospel, and I was ready to do whatever the Book said to get my sins forgiven. But that was God having mercy on a poor, depraved young man who fancied himself an intellectual. If God hadn't done something for me, my "search for God" would have ended in suicide and Hell.

Verse 7: ["And the anger of the LORD was hot against Israel, and he sold them into the hands of the Philistines, and into the hands of the children of Ammon."]{.class-0} Judgment on both sides of the Jordan: enslavement to the Philistines on the west side; enslavement to the Ammonites on the east. The time of oppression was eighteen years (vs. 8).

The main oppressor during that period, though, was Ammon. They not only attacked the Gileadites on the east side of the river, they made incursions into Judah, Benjamin, and Ephraim (vs. 9).

10 And the children of Israel cried unto the LORD, saying, We have sinned against thee, both because we have forsaken our God, and also served Baalim.

11 And the LORD said unto the children of Israel, Did not I deliver you from the Egyptians, and from the Amorites, from the children of Ammon, and from the Philistines?

12 The Zidonians also, and the Amalekites, and the Maonites, did oppress you; and ye cried to me, and I delivered you out of their hand.

13 Yet ye have forsaken me, and served other gods: wherefore I will deliver you no more.

14 Go and cry unto the gods which ye have chosen; let them deliver you in the time of your tribulation.

Verse 6 was the apostasy stage of the pattern of Judges (see our remarks under 2:16--19); verse 10 is the repentance stage. Israel cries to God for forgiveness and deliverance. Now if this were the God of modern America, He would give them a great big hug and say, "Everything's all right; everything's forgiven. I've just been waiting here on edge for you to return to me." But that's not the God of the Book.

Here, the Lord says, "I've delivered you over and over again before, but no more (vs. 13). Go to Baal and Ashtoreth and see if they'll help you. See if Chemosh and Milcom will get rid of the Ammonites for you. Go cry to the fish god (Dagon) and the fly god (Baalzebub) and see if they'll do anything about the Philistines."

["Go and cry unto the gods which ye have chosen; let them deliver you in the time of your tribulation"]{.class-0} (vs. 14).

Go cry to Obama and see if he can stop the Moslems from cutting off your oil, blowing you up, and cutting off your heads. Go ask Nancy Pelosi and see if she can pay your hospital bills when you get cancer, have to have an operation, or need six or seven medicines every day. See if Al Sharpton or Jesse Jackson can stop blacks from shooting blacks in Chicago. See if the queers, the faggots, the butches, and dykes will help your children grow up clean and doing right. That's the God of the Bible; He's not anything like the "God" of Rick Warren, the "emergents," or the seeker-sensitive movement.

15 And the children of Israel said unto the LORD, We have sinned: do thou unto us whatsoever seemeth good unto thee; deliver us only, we pray thee, this day.

16 And they put away the strange gods from among them, and served the LORD: and his soul was grieved for the misery of Israel.

The response of Israel is a good one. They are going to repent no matter what: ["We have sinned"]{.class-0} (vs. 15). They are willing to take whatever the Lord lays on them for their sin: ["do thou unto us whatsoever seemeth good unto thee."]{.class-0} They remain importunate in prayer (Luke 11:8, 18:5--7): ["Deliver us only, we pray thee, this day."]{.class-0} And they get rid of the false gods and serve the Lord (vs. 16). All of that changed the Lord's mind: ["and his soul was grieved for the misery of Israel."]{.class-0}

That should be a lesson to every "hard-shell," "primitive," five-point Calvinist. Calvin taught the decree of reprobation as well as the decree of election; that is, God arbitrarily chose before Genesis 1:1 to damn some folks as well as to save others. Well I am going to give you some good advice if you are under the decree of reprobation:[ get to Jesus Christ in repentance and faith just as quickly as you can.]{.class-1} And when you go, claim the promise He made in John 6:37--["him that cometh to me I WILL IN NO WISE CAST OUT."]{.class-0} Come to Him and call upon Him for salvation (Rom. 10:13), AND DON'T TAKE NO FOR AN ANSWER. You will get the answer to your prayer just like that Gentile woman in Matthew 15:22--28 with whom Jesus dealt outside of her dispensation (Matt. 10:5--6).

Now Christian, do you want revival? You have to admit you've sinned. Call a spade a spade. It's not a "mistake" or an "error" or a "handicap"; it's SIN. Second, completely abandon yourself to God. Throw yourself on His mercy. Next, GET RID OF THE SIN. It's not just 1 John 1:7; it's Proverbs 28:13 as well. Then, serve the Lord: DO RIGHT. That's the pattern for revival: negative first, then positive. You can't have a garden of flowers or vegetables without tearing up the ground first and pulling out the weeds.

["His soul was grieved for the misery of Israel"]{.class-0} (vs. 16). There are times when God wants to bless His people, and He can't because of their sin (Isa. 59:1--2). Jesus said of Jerusalem, ["how often would I have gathered thy children together . . . AND YE WOULD NOT!"]{.class-0} (Matt. 23:37).

The Lord said in Isaiah 48:18, ["O that thou hadst hearkened to my commandments! Then had thy peace been as a river, and thy righteousness as the waves of the sea."]{.class-0}

One time Bob Jones Sr. came back from a meeting, and he wanted to see his little boy. But Junior had done something wrong, and his mother had sent him to bed early so he couldn't see daddy when he got home. Bob Jones Sr. went up to his boy's bedroom and saw him asleep in the bed, still sniffling. Daddy could see where the tears had dried on his little boy's cheek. Bob Jones Sr. stood over that bed, looking at his boy, and he said to himself, "Isn't that something. I want to have fellowship with my little boy, and my little boy wants to have fellowship with me. But I can't have fellowship with him because he's been bad." Then looking out the window into the night sky, he said, "God, does it hurt you like this is hurting me when I do wrong?"

17 Then the children of Ammon were gathered together, and encamped in Gilead. And the children of Israel assembled themselves together, and encamped in Mizpeh.

18 And the people and princes of Gilead said one to another, What man is he that will begin to fight against the children of Ammon? he shall be head over all the inhabitants of Gilead.

The Ammonites come into Gilead to fight against the Israelites there. The Israelites come together to fight, but they have no General. They need someone who will lead the troops into battle, and whoever will do that, they'll make him the leader over all Gilead. He wouldn't be a King, but he certainly would have the power of a King. They will get their man in the next chapter, and like the other judges, he will be a type of Christ.

[]{#section-0_1_64a9c1aedcf54502_7095.xhtml}

Chapter 11

11:1 Now Jephthah the Gileadite was a mighty man of valour, and he was the son of an harlot: and Gilead begat Jephthah.

2 And Gilead's wife bare him sons; and his wife's sons grew up, and they thrust out Jephthah, and said unto him, Thou shalt not inherit in our father's house; for thou art the son of a strange woman.

3 Then Jephthah fled from his brethren, and dwelt in the land of Tob: and there were gathered vain men to Jephthah, and went out with him.

This is Jephthah, the ninth judge. Right out of the gate, he is identified as ["a mighty man of valour"]{.class-0} (vs. 1). He was a brave man in combat, but he had a bad lineage: ["he was the son of an harlot."]{.class-0} His momma was a whore, a prostitute.

Now Jephthah was a ["Gileadite."]{.class-0} The land of Gilead was named after the grandson of Manasseh, whose descendants settled on the east side of Jordan (Num. 26:29, 32:39--40). Jephthah's daddy was the namesake of his famous ancestor. There is no "contradiction." Many people are named after famous people (e.g., [George Washington]{.class-1} Carver).

Jephthah's father whored around and had a bastard son. His legitimate sons from his wife, when they were grown, got rid of Jephthah because he was a bastard from a whore. (Note the numerous references to the ["strange woman"]{.class-0} of vs. 2 in Proverbs---Prov. 2:16, 5:3, 20, 6:24, 7:5, 20:16, 23:27, 27:13. Practically, the reference is to a prostitute; prophetically, though, it refers to the Great Whore of Rev. 17.)

["Jephthah fled from his brethren"]{.class-0} (vs. 3). The implication is that if he hadn't got out of there, his brothers would have killed him. He went to live ["in the land of Tob."]{.class-0} Tob was located just outside the northernmost boundary of Gilead, on the border between Syria and Ammon.

["And there were gathered vain men to Jephthah."]{.class-0} Now like we said back in 9:4, ["vain"]{.class-0} means they were worthless and good for nothing as they were. But there is a great difference between the men who followed Abimelech and the men who followed Jephthah. The vain men who followed Abimelech were hired thugs; the vain men who followed Jephthah did so because he was a brave man. What you have here are some men who were "down and out" like the 400 men who came out to David when he was holed up in the cave of Adullam.

["And every one that was in distress, and every one that was in debt, and every one that was discontented, gathered themselves unto him; and he became a captain over them: and there were with him about four hundred men"]{.class-0} (1 Sam. 22:2).

Now I trust you understand what you're reading. Here is a man rejected by his brethren (John 1:11). He is accused of being born outside of wedlock (John 8:41). If he hadn't left, he would have been killed (Mark 8:31). He takes in those who know they're no good (John 1:12; Matt. 11:28--30; Eph. 2:8--9). [Jephthah is a type of Christ.]{.class-1}

["And went out with him."]{.class-0} This is from where the plot for "Robin Hood" comes. The Bible is the source for all the fiction plots you read in books or see in the movies and television. The historical background may change; things may be added or subtracted from the original plot so you won't recognize it. But if you boil the plot down to its basic elements, the "original" will be found in the Book.

Here is Jephthah and his crew living like outlaws in Tob. They are "going out" on raids. But they aren't robbing other Jews like the men of Shechem did to Abimelech (9:25). They're going out against Israel's enemies like David did in 1 Samuel 27:8--11.

4 And it came to pass in process of time, that the children of Ammon made war against Israel.

5 And it was so, that when the children of Ammon made war against Israel, the elders of Gilead went to fetch Jephthah out of the land of Tob:

6 And they said unto Jephthah, Come, and be our captain, that we may fight with the children of Ammon.

7 And Jephthah said unto the elders of Gilead, Did not ye hate me, and expel me out of my father's house? and why are ye come unto me now when ye are in distress?

8 And the elders of Gilead said unto Jephthah, Therefore we turn again to thee now, that thou mayest go with us, and fight against the children of Ammon, and be our head over all the inhabitants of Gilead.

9 And Jephthah said unto the elders of Gilead, If ye bring me home again to fight against the children of Ammon, and the LORD deliver them before me, shall I be your head?

10 And the elders of Gilead said unto Jephthah, The LORD be witness between us, if we do not so according to thy words.

11 Then Jephthah went with the elders of Gilead, and the people made him head and captain over them: and Jephthah uttered all his words before the LORD in Mizpeh.

The passage immediately follows 10:17--18. The Gileadites are looking for someone to lead them into battle against the Ammonites, and they remember their kin whom they threw out. They send an embassage to Jephthah and beg him to come down and lead them in the fight against Ammon. If the truth be told, they still wanted nothing to do with him, but they knew full well that he was the only man who could handle the job.

Of course, Jephthah knew how they really felt: ["Did not ye hate me . . . ?" ]{.class-0}(vs. 7). Yeah, they did, but their need overrode their hatred. They were willing to make him the head of all the Gileadites if he would lead them to victory in battle (vs. 8).

Again, you are looking at a type of Christ. You see it in the greatest type of Christ in the Bible, Joseph. Joseph's brothers hated him until they were ["in distress"]{.class-0} from a famine; then they were bowing down to him and begging his forgiveness. The Jews, as a nation, hated Christ (John 15:24--25). That hatred persists to the point where they are ["in distress"]{.class-0} from the Antichrist; then it's ["they shall look upon me whom they have pierced, and they shall mourn for him, as one mourneth for his only son"]{.class-0} (Zech. 12:10). That's when they accept Christ as their King, and not until.

Jephthah is naturally suspicious of the elders' sincerity, so they make a promise to God to keep their side of the bargain. Jephthah goes with the elders of Gilead to Mizpeh, where the Gileadites are encamped in preparation for war with the Ammonites (10:17). There he is "sworn in" as the head of all Gilead. Jephthah, in turn, ["uttered all his words before the LORD in Mizpeh"]{.class-0} (vs. 11). Jephthah made some kind of vow to the Lord there in Mizpeh. The vow is in verses 30--31.

"And Jephthah vowed a vow unto the LORD, and said, If thou shalt without fail deliver the children of Ammon into mine hands, Then it shall be, that whatsoever cometh forth of the doors of my house to meet me, when I return in peace from the children of Ammon, shall surely be the LORD'S, and I will offer it up for a burnt offering."

["The doors of my house,"]{.class-0} in this instance, is a reference to [the gates of his property.]{.class-1} He's saying, "Whatever comes out of the yard first when I get home from whipping the Ammonites, I'll offer it for a burnt offering to the Lord." He's expecting a rooster or an ox or a sheep or some beast like that to wander out, and that's not what happens, as we shall see when we get to verse 34.

12 And Jephthah sent messengers unto the king of the children of Ammon, saying, What hast thou to do with me, that thou art come against me to fight in my land?

13 And the king of the children of Ammon answered unto the messengers of Jephthah, Because Israel took away my land, when they came up out of Egypt, from Arnon even unto Jabbok, and unto Jordan: now therefore restore those lands again peaceably.

14 And Jephthah sent messengers again unto the king of the children of Ammon:

15 And said unto him, Thus saith Jephthah, Israel took not away the land of Moab, nor the land of the children of Ammon:

16 But when Israel came up from Egypt, and walked through the wilderness unto the Red sea, and came to Kadesh;

17 Then Israel sent messengers unto the king of Edom, saying, Let me, I pray thee, pass through thy land: but the king of Edom would not hearken thereto. And in like manner they sent unto the king of Moab: but he would not consent: and Israel abode in Kadesh.

18 Then they went along through the wilderness, and compassed the land of Edom, and the land of Moab, and came by the east side of the land of Moab, and pitched on the other side of Arnon, but came not within the border of Moab: for Arnon was the border of Moab.

19 And Israel sent messengers unto Sihon king of the Amorites, the king of Heshbon; and Israel said unto him, Let us pass, we pray thee, through thy land into my place.

20 But Sihon trusted not Israel to pass through his coast: but Sihon gathered all his people together, and pitched in Jahaz, and fought against Israel.

21 And the LORD God of Israel delivered Sihon and all his people into the hand of Israel, and they smote them: so Israel possessed all the land of the Amorites, the inhabitants of that country.

22 And they possessed all the coasts of the Amorites, from Arnon even unto Jabbok, and from the wilderness even unto Jordan.

Now Jephthah doesn't attack Ammon from the "get go"; he tries diplomacy first. He sends messengers to the King of Ammon, asking, "What's your 'beef' with us, man? What's so important that it's worth going to war over?" The King's answer was: "I'm just taking back what belongs to me. When you people came in here, you took all my land from Arnon to the Jabbok River."

[That wasn't true.]{.class-1} When Israel ended its forty years of wandering in the wilderness, they were coming up to the east side of the Jordan to enter the land under Joshua (see Josh. 3). When they did, there were three lands there God wouldn't let them touch. One was the land of Edom (Deut. 2:4--5), another was Moab (Deut. 2:9), and the third was Ammon.

["And when thou comest nigh over against the children of Ammon, distress them not, nor meddle with them: for I will not give thee of the land of the children of Ammon any possession; because I have given it unto the children of Lot for a possession"]{.class-0} (Deut. 2:19).

Edom was the land of Esau, Jacob's brother, and Moab and Ammon were the bastard sons of Lot through incest with his daughters (Gen. 19:36--38). Because those people were related to Abraham, God gave those lands to those peoples. When Israel was coming to the crossing of Jordan, they had to ask permission to pass through those lands, and they had to pay for any food or water they consumed along the way. In each instance, they were turned down, so they had to go out of their way to go around those lands because they weren't allowed by God to take anything from those nations.

But when the Israelites came to the two Amorite kingdoms of Sihon and Og in the lands of Gilead and Bashan, north of Ammon and Moab, God allowed the Jews to go in there and wipe out the Amorites and take their lands.

Now what was the problem with the Ammonites? Well, at one point, Ammon [had]{.class-1} owned all the land from Arnon to the Jabbok, but long before the children of Israel ever showed up, the Moabites had taken that land from the Ammonites. Then the Amorites took that land away from the Moabites. You can read the historical record in Numbers 21:23--29. So when the children of Israel conquered Sihon's land, they got the land from Arnon to the Jabbok: they got it from the [Amorites,]{.class-1} not the [Ammonites.]{.class-1} Israel never touched one square inch of Ammon's land.

23 So now the LORD God of Israel hath dispossessed the Amorites from before his people Israel, and shouldest thou possess it?

24 Wilt not thou possess that which Chemosh thy god giveth thee to possess? So whomsoever the LORD our God shall drive out from before us, them will we possess.

25 And now art thou any thing better than Balak the son of Zippor, king of Moab? did he ever strive against Israel, or did he ever fight against them,

26 While Israel dwelt in Heshbon and her towns, and in Aroer and her towns, and in all the cities that be along by the coasts of Arnon, three hundred years? why therefore did ye not recover them within that time?

27 Wherefore I have not sinned against thee, but thou doest me wrong to war against me: the LORD the Judge be judge this day between the children of Israel and the children of Ammon.

28 Howbeit the king of the children of Ammon hearkened not unto the words of Jephthah which he sent him.

So what's Jephthah's point? Well, God had taken away the Amorites land and had given it to Israel. Ammon was trying to possess the land [God]{.class-1} had given to Israel. They were trying to defy the Creator of the universe just like Hamas, the Muslim Brotherhood, the UN, and the [United States]{.class-1} is trying to do right now.

["Wilt not thou possess that which Chemosh thy god giveth thee to possess? So whomsoever the LORD our God shall drive out from before us, them will we possess"]{.class-0} (vs. 24). The idea is if Chemosh was such a big boy, why couldn't he prevent Sihon from taking away the Ammonites' land? Jehovah was certainly able to take away Sihon's land and give it to Israel.

["And now art thou any thing better than Balak the son of Zippor, king of Moab? did he ever strive against Israel, did he ever fight against them"]{.class-0} (vs. 25). Yeah, he did; he was the one who hired Balaam to curse Israel (Josh. 24:9). God wouldn't listen to Balak any more than He was going to listen to the King of Ammon here (Josh. 24:10).

Now, the Ammonites had had 300 years to take back the land if it were actually theirs, and they hadn't lifted a finger to do it (vs. 26). Jephthah's conclusion is: ["I have not sinned against thee, but thou doest me wrong to war against me"]{.class-0} (vs. 27). Of course the Ammonite King doesn't pay any attention to Jephthah's argument (vs. 28), so there is going to be war.

["The LORD the Judge"]{.class-0} (vs. 27). Now that's what the lost world can't stand about God. They don't mind the cosmic "Grandpa" or "Santa Claus" who just loves everybody and only does good to everybody. What they can't stand is that ["Judge of all the earth"]{.class-0} who will always ["do right"]{.class-0} (Gen. 18:25), to whom they will have to give an account some day (Rom. 14:12).

That Bible says that ["Judge"]{.class-0} is a completely ["righteous judge"]{.class-0} (2 Tim. 4:8); [He won't do wrong to get you out of Hell.]{.class-1} As a ["righteous judge,"]{.class-0} He ["is angry with the wicked every day"]{.class-0} (Psa. 7:11). As the ["judge of the earth,"]{.class-0} He will ["render a reward to the proud"]{.class-0} (Psa. 94:2); He will put down certain nations and lift up others based on His own righteous judgment (Psa. 75:7). One day when He returns to ["inherit all nations,"]{.class-0} He will ["judge the earth"]{.class-0} (Psa. 82:8 cf. Matt. 25:31--46).

In the meantime, the individual sinner should keep in mind that God will someday judge [everything]{.class-1} he does (Eccl. 11:9, 12:14), and He will do it [by His Son]{.class-1} (Rom. 2:16), whom He raised from the dead (Acts 17:31). Thank God I will never have to face that judgment. My sins were all judged at Calvary when Jesus Christ became sin in my place (2 Cor. 5:21) and took the curse of the Law, which I should have suffered, on Himself (Gal. 3:13). When I trusted the sacrifice the Judge made for me (Isa. 53:10), I not only didn't have my sins counted to me (Rom. 4:8), I got the Judge's righteousness put on my account so that I am completely just before the Law (Rom. 4:5). WELL GLORY! If that's not true of you, the only reason is that you haven't been to the Judge yet for your reprieve (John 5:40).

29 Then the Spirit of the LORD came upon Jephthah, and he passed over Gilead, and Manasseh, and passed over Mizpeh of Gilead, and from Mizpeh of Gilead he passed over unto the children of Ammon.

30 And Jephthah vowed a vow unto the LORD, and said, If thou shalt without fail deliver the children of Ammon into mine hands,

31 Then it shall be, that whatsoever cometh forth of the doors of my house to meet me, when I return in peace from the children of Ammon, shall surely be the LORD'S, and I will offer it up for a burnt offering.

32 So Jephthah passed over unto the children of Ammon to fight against them; and the LORD delivered them into his hands.

33 And he smote them from Aroer, even till thou come to Minnith, even twenty cities, and unto the plain of the vineyards, with a very great slaughter. Thus the children of Ammon were subdued before the children of Israel.

34 And Jephthah came to Mizpeh unto his house, and, behold, his daughter came out to meet him with timbrels and with dances: and she was his only child; beside her he had neither son nor daughter.

This is what is known as "Jephthah's rash vow": he promised something to God without considering all it might entail. The Book says, ["Be not rash with thy mouth, and let not thine heart be hasty to utter any thing before God . . . let thy words be few"]{.class-0} (Eccl. 5:2). Why? [Because God expects you to pay what you vowed]{.class-1} (Eccl. 5:4). You would be better off not to promise anything to God rather than to break the promise (Eccl. 5:5).

Jephthah was short-sighted when it came to what would come out to meet him on his return. He thought it would be a beast; [it was his own daughter,]{.class-1} his only child. According to verse 39, Jephthah kept his vow: he offered his daughter ["for a burnt offering"]{.class-0} (vs. 31).

Now if you go to any major Fundamental school in this country, they don't believe that. I have a syllabus from Bob Jones University for their class on Judges. It says Jephthah sacrificed a rooster, and the only sacrifice his daughter had to make was to remain unmarried. Would anyone care to show me from Scripture where either of those statements is so? Don't bet any money on it. After Jephthah's daughter ["bewailed her virginity"]{.class-0} (vs. 38), Jephthah ["did with her according to his vow"]{.class-0} (vs. 39). [Remaining a virgin had nothing to do with the vow.]{.class-1} How about making the rooster a perpetual virgin!

35 And it came to pass, when he saw her, that he rent his clothes, and said, Alas, my daughter! thou hast brought me very low, and thou art one of them that trouble me: for I have opened my mouth unto the LORD, and I cannot go back.

36 And she said unto him, My father, if thou hast opened thy mouth unto the LORD, do to me according to that which hath proceeded out of thy mouth; forasmuch as the LORD hath taken vengeance for thee of thine enemies, even of the children of Ammon.

37 And she said unto her father, Let this thing be done for me: let me alone two months, that I may go up and down upon the mountains, and bewail my virginity, I and my fellows.

38 And he said, Go. And he sent her away for two months: and she went with her companions, and bewailed her virginity upon the mountains.

39 And it came to pass at the end of two months, that she returned unto her father, who did with her according to his vow which he had vowed: and she knew no man. And it was a custom in Israel,

40 That the daughters of Israel went yearly to lament the daughter of Jephthah the Gileadite four days in a year.

["Alas, my daughter! thou hast brought me very low"]{.class-0} (vs. 35). It just about kills Jephthah to see his daughter come out. Why in the world would he rend his clothes (a symbol that he wanted to [die]{.class-1}) if all he intended to do was keep his daughter a virgin? She may never have married or bore him grandchildren, [but she would still be alive.]{.class-1}

["I have opened my mouth unto the LORD, and I cannot go back."]{.class-0} Jephthah is determined to follow Ecclesiastes 5:4--6 to its bitter end. To withhold a married life from his only child was not even a part of what he vowed in verse 31.

Now grace had a remedy for Jephthah's stupid promise, but [ignorance of the Scripture]{.class-1} (cf. Matt. 22:29) kept it from him. Have you noticed in the last eleven chapters that there has been no reference to anybody picking up the Mosaic Book of the Law (see Josh. 1:8) to read it, teach it, preach it, or follow it? In the days of the judges, ["every man did that which was right in his own eyes"]{.class-0} (17:6, 21:25).

Jephthah figured it was right to keep his vow to the Lord, and he was right up to a point. But if he had been reading his Bible, he could have found a way out of his oath. According to Leviticus 5:4--10, if a man made a vow that would cause him to sin (like murdering his daughter), he could confess the sin of making such a vow and bring an offering to the priest for an atonement. [It would not be a rooster.]{.class-1} The sacrifice was to be a female lamb or a female kid, or ["two turtledoves, or two young pigeons"]{.class-0} (Lev. 5:7). Jephthah's failure to study his Bible and hide God's word in his heart (Psa. 119:11) cost him the life of his daughter. Such failure has cost many a modern American much more than that.

Now suppose you are a Mason or a Jesuit who has made a vow to God to murder and mutilate others in service to lodge or church. If you get saved, you are under no obligation to stay in such a hellish organization just because you made a promise that you had no idea of what it fully meant at the time. You have a [better]{.class-1} sacrifice than ["the blood of bulls and of goats"]{.class-0} (Heb. 10:4). ["The blood of Jesus Christ his Son cleanseth us from ALL sin"]{.class-0} (1 John 1:7). Confess your rash vow (1 John 1:9), get it under the blood (1 John 1:7), and then [do right]{.class-1} and get out of that organization (2 Cor. 6:17).

The Lord let Jephthah do the wrong thing and didn't stop him. God is not for human sacrifice (Lev. 18:21; Deut. 18:10), but God will let you reap the consequences of your sin if you ignore His word. That's what happened to Jephthah.

["And she said unto him, My father, if thou hast opened thy mouth unto the LORD, do to me according to that which hath proceeded out of thy mouth"]{.class-0} (vs. 36). No bait and switch with this sister. She says, "Daddy, if you promised to burn me, then burn me." Offered a rooster my foot!

Young man, if you get yourself a girl like that for a wife, you'll have yourself something more than a clothing store dummy. That young lady had the glory of God in mind: she put God first. She was happier over the fact that God's enemies had been whipped than she was about keeping her own life. She considered God's glory more important than her own life. See the New Testament application in Luke 14:26 and our comments in that Commentary.

["Let me alone two months, that I may go up and down upon the mountains, and bewail my virginity"]{.class-0} (vs. 37). Here's from where all the nonsense comes about Jephthah keeping his vow by keeping his daughter a virgin. An honest reader of the passage realizes she is bewailing the fact that she will never be married and have children [because she is about to die.]{.class-1}

Why would a bunch of women go [yearly for four days]{.class-1} to lament the virginity of one woman? One commentator says Jephthah's daughter was consecrated "to the service of God in the tabernacle, in a state of celibacy." Why would serving God in any "state" be something to ["lament,"]{.class-0} much less on a yearly basis? Hannah didn't lament turning Samuel over to service in the Tabernacle. Far from "lamenting," she [rejoiced]{.class-1} (1 Sam. 2:1). ["The daughters of Israel"]{.class-0} (vs. 40) were lamenting that Jephthah's daughter died in the prime of her life without having been married. Without the death of the girl, the passage is nonsense.

[]{#section-0_1_b8a2d4a4c25a2932_7096.xhtml}

Chapter 12

12:1 And the men of Ephraim gathered themselves together, and went northward, and said unto Jephthah, Wherefore passedst thou over to fight against the children of Ammon, and didst not call us to go with thee? we will burn thine house upon thee with fire.

2 And Jephthah said unto them, I and my people were at great strife with the children of Ammon; and when I called you, ye delivered me not out of their hands.

3 And when I saw that ye delivered me not, I put my life in my hands, and passed over against the children of Ammon, and the LORD delivered them into my hand: wherefore then are ye come up unto me this day, to fight against me?

4 Then Jephthah gathered together all the men of Gilead, and fought with Ephraim: and the men of Gilead smote Ephraim, because they said, Ye Gileadites are fugitives of Ephraim among the Ephraimites, and among the Manassites.

5 And the Gileadites took the passages of Jordan before the Ephraimites: and it was so, that when those Ephraimites which were escaped said, Let me go over; that the men of Gilead said unto him, Art thou an Ephraimite? If he said, Nay;

6 Then said they unto him, Say now Shibboleth: and he said Sibboleth: for he could not frame to pronounce it right. Then they took him, and slew him at the passages of Jordan: and there fell at that time of the Ephraimites forty and two thousand.

7 And Jephthah judged Israel six years. Then died Jephthah the Gileadite, and was buried in one of the cities of Gilead.

The Ephraimites once more try pulling the stunt with Jephthah that they pulled off with Gideon back in 7:1--3---"Why didn't you call us to the battle?" They are seeking their own glory. But this time they add a threat in their arrogance that they did not use on Gideon: ["we will burn thine house upon thee with fire"]{.class-0} (vs. 1).

Wrong thing to say to a man like Jephthah who is preparing to burn his daughter---the wrong thing to say at the wrong time. Unlike their Manassite counterparts on the west side of Jordan, Jephthah and the Gileadites aren't going to put up with Ephraim's foolishness.

["When I called you, ye delivered me not out of their hands"]{.class-0} (vs. 2). So the Ephraimites lied. Jephthah did call them, and they didn't come. Talk about adding insult to injury, the Ephraimites are cooking their own goose.

The Ephraimites want a fight, so Jephthah gives them one: ["Then Jephthah gathered together all the men of Gilead, and fought with Ephraim: and the men of Gilead smote Ephraim"]{.class-0} (vs. 4). Forty-two thousand of them end up dying (vs. 6).

["Because they said, Ye Gileadites are fugitives of Ephraim among the Ephraimites, and among the Manassites"]{.class-0} (vs. 4). The Gileadites are finally getting a taste of what they put Jephthah through: "You don't belong; you're not part of the family." The Gileadites were the half tribe of Manasseh that settled on the east side of Jordan.

To prevent the Ephraimites from escaping across Jordan to their own territory, the men of Gilead take the fords of Jordan at Bethbarah. To make sure no Ephraimite gets across, the Gileadites create a "password." Whoever wanted to cross Jordan had to say the word ["Shibboleth"]{.class-0} (vs. 6). The problem was the Ephraimites, being from a different part of Israel, could not pronounce the "sh" sound; it came out as "Sibboleth." It would be like requiring a New England Yankee to say the word [car;]{.class-1} it would come out as "caw" nearly every time. To this day the word [Shibboleth]{.class-1} has become synonymous with being pickish and particular as to how a word is pronounced.

The idea is that there are some words that different people have a terrible time pronouncing. Europeans have a problem with an English "th"; they will pronounce it as a "d." Americans have a hard time with German gutturals. During World War II, in the Pacific Theater, a password like [lollapalooza]{.class-1} was picked because the Japanese couldn't pronounce the "l" sound; they would say "rorraparooza."

["And Jephthah judged Israel six years."]{.class-0} Of all the judges to date who had the time of their terms recorded, Jephthah had one of the shortest: six years. He is followed by three more judges who all have short terms. During this time, there is no mention of oppression or enslavement; this seems to be a time of peace where these judges lead Israel to do right.

8 And after him Ibzan of Bethlehem judged Israel.

9 And he had thirty sons, and thirty daughters, whom he sent abroad, and took in thirty daughters from abroad for his sons. And he judged Israel seven years.

10 Then died Ibzan, and was buried at Bethlehem.

11 And after him Elon, a Zebulonite, judged Israel; and he judged Israel ten years.

12 And Elon the Zebulonite died, and was buried in Aijalon in the country of Zebulun.

13 And after him Abdon the son of Hillel, a Pirathonite, judged Israel.

14 And he had forty sons and thirty nephews, that rode on threescore and ten ass colts: and he judged Israel eight years.

15 And Abdon the son of Hillel the Pirathonite died, and was buried in Pirathon in the land of Ephraim, in the mount of the Amalekites.

Ibzan (vss. 9--10) is the tenth judge. The ["Bethlehem"]{.class-0} in verse 8 is probably the one in Zebulun (see Josh. 19:15), although the Jewish Rabbis try to equate Ibzan with Boaz. His term as judge was seven years (vs. 9).

["He had thirty sons, and thirty daughters, whom he sent abroad, and took in thirty daughters from abroad for his sons"]{.class-0} (vs. 9). Unlike Jephthah, Ibzan had quite a family ([sixty]{.class-1} children), and undoubtedly this was due to him having multiple wives (see Gideon's situation in 8:30). Judging strictly by the context, when the verse says Ibzan sent his daughters ["abroad,"]{.class-0} it would refer to him sending his daughters outside of their hometown of Bethlehem to get husbands (cf. ["took in thirty daughters from abroad for his sons"]{.class-0} ). Some of the expositors, though, will run you to verse 14 here in chapter 12 and to 10:4 to show you that the judges who had large families often sent their children out to other areas of Israel as judges.

Elon (vss. 11--12) is the eleventh judge. He also is from Zebulun, and his term is ten years. Outside of that and his burial place in verse 12, that is all we are told about him.

I used to wonder why God would put such things like that in the Bible. All right, Elon was a judge and ruled ten years. So what? What did he do? You aren't told. What were his accomplishments? There's nothing recorded. God puts stuff like that in a Book that will last into eternity (Matt. 24:35). It's kind of like that fellow in Genesis 36:24 whose sole "claim to fame" was finding some ["mules in the wilderness."]{.class-0} Big deal!

But you see, God is interested in His Son, and anyone connected with His Son, God recognizes, even if it is for something as insignificant as finding some wild jackasses or something as seemingly mundane as serving ten years "on the bench." Do you want God to show some interest in you? [Then show some interest in His Son.]{.class-1}

["And after him Abdon"]{.class-0} (vs. 13). This is the twelfth judge. He is from ["Pirathon in the land of Ephraim"]{.class-0} (vs. 15). Some commentators equate him with the ["Bedan"]{.class-0} of 1 Samuel 12:11, although what a minor judge like Abdon would be doing listed among major judges like Jephthah, Gideon, and Samuel is never explained. Like Jair back in 10:4, he sends his sons, along with his nephews, out as judges, which is symbolized by the ["ass colts"]{.class-0} they ride (see our comments on 10:4 and 5:10). Abdon ["judged Israel eight years"]{.class-0} (vs. 14), and again, because the land is at rest in his days, there is nothing particularly outstanding or exciting about him or his rule as a judge.

[]{#section-0_1_badd970cd7e59e1d_7097.xhtml}

Chapter 13

13:1 And the children of Israel did evil again in the sight of the LORD; and the LORD delivered them into the hand of the Philistines forty years.

2 And there was a certain man of Zorah, of the family of the Danites, whose name was Manoah; and his wife was barren, and bare not.

3 And the angel of the LORD appeared unto the woman, and said unto her, Behold now, thou art barren, and bearest not: but thou shalt conceive, and bear a son.

Now between Jephthah and three minor judges, the Israelites have been through 31 years of peace in the land, but with chapter 13 (and watch out for the [thirteen]{.class-1}), that time of rest ends. The people fall back into disobedience and idolatry, and the Lord sends in the Philistines to oppress them forty years (vs. 1). So God is getting ready to send a major judge, one who will not just judge the nation like Tola, Jair, Ibzan, Elon, and Abdon, but also deliver the people like Othniel, Ehud, Barak, Gideon, and Jephthah.

But this judge is going to be different from all previous judges. First of all, he is the only judge chosen to be a judge [before he was born.]{.class-1} Second, he will be the only judge who does not fully deliver Israel from its oppressors. He is the [thirteenth]{.class-1} judge in the book of Judges, and you had better watch out for the thirteen (see our comments on the number in our work [Bible Numerics]{.class-1}). This will be Samson. He is from the tribe of Dan (vs. 2), the tribe that apostatizes and really never does get right (see 18:30).

As the chapter opens, Samson's mother is barren. She is one of seven women in the Bible who typify Mary, the mother of Jesus, which makes their sons types of Christ. There is Sarah who gives birth to Isaac, who becomes one of the clearest pictures in the Bible of Christ's sacrifice and His resurrection (Heb. 11:17--19). There is Rebekah who gives birth to Jacob, Israel---the nation that is called God's firstborn son (Exod. 4:22). There is Rachel who gives birth to Joseph, a type of Christ in over 150 particulars. There is Manoah's wife here, the mother of Samson; her son is a judge, a type of Christ as Deliverer at the Second Advent (Rom. 11:26). There is Hannah, the mother of Samuel, who pictured Christ in the office of Prophet and Priest, as well as His intercessory ministry. There is the Shunamite woman in 2 Kings 4 whose son was raised from the dead. And there is Elizabeth in Luke 1, who gave birth to John the Baptist, the forerunner of Jesus Christ.

4 Now therefore beware, I pray thee, and drink not wine nor strong drink, and eat not any unclean thing:

5 For, lo, thou shalt conceive, and bear a son; and no razor shall come on his head: for the child shall be a Nazarite unto God from the womb: and he shall begin to deliver Israel out of the hand of the Philistines.

This is the Nazarite of Numbers 6. Normally, a Nazarite consecrated himself for a certain period of time in which he fulfilled a vow he had taken. Samson was to be a Nazarite from his mother's womb all the days of his life.

The rules for a Nazarite were that he was not to eat or drink anything that came from a grape (see our comments on the forbidden fruit under Judg. 9:8--13). That restriction was put on Manoah's wife because Samson was to be a Nazarite even in the womb. He wasn't to touch a dead body (Num. 6:6), and he wasn't to cut his hair. A stipulation that is implied in Numbers 6 but is not outright stated is that he wasn't to ["eat . . . any unclean thing"]{.class-0} (vs. 4). Numbers 6:8 says, ["All the days of his separation he is holy unto the LORD."]{.class-0}

["And he shall BEGIN to deliver Israel out of the hand of the Philistines"]{.class-0} (vs. 5). As was observed earlier, Samson is the only judge in the book who does not fully deliver Israel from its heathen oppressors. Whether this was God's intention or whether that last clause in verse 5 is merely a prophetic observation is unclear. As things work out, Samson is distracted from delivering Israel by his [sins.]{.class-1} In fact, God has to use Samson's lusts to get him to kill the Philistines (see 14:4). When all is said and done, it is Samuel and David who end up delivering Israel from the Philistines (1 Sam. 7:13; 2 Sam. 8:1).

6 Then the woman came and told her husband, saying, A man of God came unto me, and his countenance was like the countenance of an angel of God, very terrible: but I asked him not whence he was, neither told he me his name:

7 But he said unto me, Behold, thou shalt conceive, and bear a son; and now drink no wine nor strong drink, neither eat any unclean thing: for the child shall be a Nazarite to God from the womb to the day of his death.

8 Then Manoah intreated the LORD, and said, O my Lord, let the man of God which thou didst send come again unto us, and teach us what we shall do unto the child that shall be born.

Now here is a woman out in her yard. She is all by herself when suddenly an angel of light appears to her speaking of forbidden fruit. Do you realize what you're reading? You're reading the counterpart of Genesis 3.

When Satan appeared to Eve, I imagine he appeared as ["an angel of light"]{.class-0} (2 Cor. 11:14). You say, "Then why does the Bible call him a serpent?" Because Moses wrote Genesis, and Moses knew what the Devil was (Rev. 12:9). Brethren, the Devil never comes to you looking like a serpent; he comes at you as something appealing: ["an angel of light."]{.class-0}

Manoah's wife, having more sense than Eve, doesn't deal with the angel on her own. She tells her husband, who then deals with him. Ladies, whether you like it or not, God's plan is the woman in subjection to her husband, the man in subjection to Christ, and Christ in subjection to the Father (1 Tim. 2:11; 1 Cor. 14:34--35; 1 Cor. 11:3). I know that sin and laziness on the part of the man can mess that order up many a time, but don't you make a mess of things, sister, with your pride and ambition.

Three things more before moving on to the next passage. First, notice how the word ["terrible"]{.class-0} is used in verse 6. It doesn't mean something [extremely bad;]{.class-1} it is used in the sense of [inspiring terror.]{.class-1} When the children of Israel saw God descend in fire, smoke, and lightning on Mt. Sinai, they were terrified (Heb. 12:21). Paul says, ["Knowing the terror of the Lord, we persuade men"]{.class-0} (2 Cor. 5:11). If you have any sense, you'd be terrified too of a completely holy, sinless Being who could create a furnace like the sun.

Second, notice what the Angel of the Lord is called in verse 6: ["A MAN of God."]{.class-0} Notice it again in verses 8, 10, 11. Despite the teaching of the [Scofield Reference Bible]{.class-1} in Genesis 6:4 that angels are sexless, the clear statements in Scripture are that angels are [male;]{.class-1} there is not a "sexless" angel in that Book. Jacob wrestled with ["a man"]{.class-0} (Gen. 32:24) who was the Angel of the Lord (Gen. 22:30; Hos. 12:3--4). Revelation 21:17 defines an angel as ["a man."]{.class-0} In the Bible (the [text,]{.class-1} not the Scofield notes), an angel is a [33½-year-old male]{.class-1} (cf. 1 John 3:2) [without wings.]{.class-1}

Finally, note the term[ "man of God"]{.class-0} in verses 7--8. The first time it shows up in Scripture, it is a reference to Moses (Deut. 33:1). Moses was a [prophet]{.class-1} (Deut. 18:18), and that is how the term ["man of God"]{.class-0} is used throughout the Old Testament (see 1 Sam. 2:27, 9:6--10; 1 Kings 12:22, 13:1, 4--8, 17:18, 24, 20:28; 2 Kings 1:9--13, 4:7, 9; 2 Chron. 8:14, 25:7, 9; Neh. 12:24, 36; Jer. 35:4; etc.). The Angel of the Lord here is [prophesying]{.class-1} of the birth of Samson; hence the term ["man of God."]{.class-0} But more than that, in the Old Testament, the Angel of the Lord was a pre-incarnate appearance of the Lord Jesus Christ (Acts 27:23; Gal. 4:14)---the God-[man.]{.class-1} This is the only passage in the Bible where an angel is called a ["man of God,"]{.class-0} and it is because it is Jesus Christ as the Angel of the Lord exercising His office as Prophet.

9 And God hearkened to the voice of Manoah; and the angel of God came again unto the woman as she sat in the field: but Manoah her husband was not with her.

10 And the woman made haste, and ran, and shewed her husband, and said unto him, Behold, the man hath appeared unto me, that came unto me the other day.

11 And Manoah arose, and went after his wife, and came to the man, and said unto him, Art thou the man that spakest unto the woman? And he said, I am.

12 And Manoah said, Now let thy words come to pass. How shall we order the child, and how shall we do unto him?

13 And the angel of the LORD said unto Manoah, Of all that I said unto the woman let her beware.

14 She may not eat of any thing that cometh of the vine, neither let her drink wine or strong drink, nor eat any unclean thing: all that I commanded her let her observe.

As the head of his wife, Manoah has the right to ["try the spirits"]{.class-0} to see ["whether they are of God"]{.class-0} (1 John 4:1). In the Old Testament, the husband had the power to cancel his wife's vow if he disallowed it the moment he found out about it; otherwise, it stood (Num. 30:6--8). Manoah prays for the angel to be sent back so he can find out if it is the Angel of the Lord or the serpent of Genesis 3 masquerading as ["an angel of light."]{.class-0}

God answers Manoah's prayer, but the angel doesn't appear to Manoah; he comes back to the woman. She had to go fetch her husband and bring him to the angel. Good woman: she's operating the way she should. Whether it were God or the Devil, it was the husband's responsibility to handle, not hers.

Verse 13: ["And the angel of the LORD said unto Manoah, Of all that I said unto the woman let her beware."]{.class-0} The Lord told Moses that the children of Israel were to beware of the Angel God sent before them into the Promised Land ["and obey his voice"]{.class-0} (Exod. 23:20--24). In the New Testament, the Father said, ["This is my beloved Son, in whom I am well pleased; hear ye him"]{.class-0} (Matt. 17:5). The ["hear"]{.class-0} there is hearing [with obedience]{.class-1} (cf. John 12:47). Jesus said if you were ["of God,"]{.class-0} you would hear God's words in that manner (John 8:47). As a Christian, you are to ["observe"]{.class-0} (vs. 14) ["all things"]{.class-0} that your Saviour commanded you (Matt. 28:20).

15 And Manoah said unto the angel of the LORD, I pray thee, let us detain thee, until we shall have made ready a kid for thee.

16 And the angel of the LORD said unto Manoah, Though thou detain me, I will not eat of thy bread: and if thou wilt offer a burnt offering, thou must offer it unto the LORD. For Manoah knew not that he was an angel of the LORD.

17 And Manoah said unto the angel of the LORD, What is thy name, that when thy sayings come to pass we may do thee honour?

18 And the angel of the LORD said unto him, Why askest thou thus after my name, seeing it is secret?

19 So Manoah took a kid with a meat offering, and offered it upon a rock unto the LORD: and the angel did wondrously; and Manoah and his wife looked on.

20 For it came to pass, when the flame went up toward heaven from off the altar, that the angel of the LORD ascended in the flame of the altar. And Manoah and his wife looked on it, and fell on their faces to the ground.

21 But the angel of the LORD did no more appear to Manoah and to his wife. Then Manoah knew that he was an angel of the LORD.

Notice that up to this point, ["Manoah knew not that he was an angel of the LORD"]{.class-0} (vs. 16). This is a perfect illustration of Hebrews 13:2.

"Be not forgetful to entertain strangers: for thereby some have entertained angels unawares."

If a woman about seven-foot tall with long blond hair and wings up to eight feet long each showed up at your door, do you reckon you would be "aware" that the creature was something other than a "man"? You see now why we told you that [angels have no wings.]{.class-1} They can come and go as men without raising any eyebrows until they reveal the power God has given them (see Gen. 19).

Now Manoah offers the man before him the hospitality of a meal (vs. 15). The angel turns him down and tells him that any burnt offering must be offered to the Lord. Now if this is a pre-incarnate appearance of God the Son, then why didn't he accept the offering? Because He, as the Second Person of the Godhead, was to [be]{.class-1} the sacrifice (Isa. 53:10). When He was here on earth as a man, He said, ["the Son of man came not to be ministered unto, but to minister, and to give his life a ransom for many"]{.class-0} (Mark 10:45).

Manoah then asked the man, ["What is thy name, that when thy sayings come to pass we may do thee honour?"]{.class-0} (vs. 17). Manoah wants to name the baby after the prophet. The cross-references to the question are found in Genesis 32:29, where Jacob asks the angel's name who wrestled with him, and Proverbs 30:4, where the writer says of the Creator, ["what is his name, and what is HIS SON'S NAME, if thou canst tell?"]{.class-0} But the reason you aren't told His name in the Old Testament is because, at that time, it was ["secret"]{.class-0} (vs. 18). It wasn't to be revealed until ["the fulness of the time was come"]{.class-0} (Gal. 4:4).

Of course, the careful reader of Scripture will notice what nearly all the commentators missed. The ["secret"]{.class-0} name [is given]{.class-1} in the context of Genesis 32:29; Judges 13:17--18; and Proverbs 30:4. In Genesis 32:30, the name is ["God"]{.class-0} (cf. 1 John 5:20). In Judges 13:19, it is ["wondrously"]{.class-0} (cf. Isa. 9:6). In Proverbs 30:5, it is the ["word of God"]{.class-0} (cf. John 1:1--2; 1 John 1:1--2). The name that is ["secret"]{.class-0} was the name He would bear as a man.

In verse 19, Manoah makes an offering to the Lord like Gideon did in 6:19--20, and in verse 20, Manoah finally finds out that the ["man"]{.class-0} to whom he has been talking is the Angel of the Lord. As the fire leaps up from the sacrifice, that fellow jumps up on the altar and goes up to Heaven in the flames just as pretty as you please without any third-degree burns. Needless to say, Manoah and his wife had the fire scared out of them. They are flat on their faces on the ground in terror, but that was the last time they saw the Angel of the Lord.

22 And Manoah said unto his wife, We shall surely die, because we have seen God.

23 But his wife said unto him, If the LORD were pleased to kill us, he would not have received a burnt offering and a meat offering at our hands, neither would he have shewed us all these things, nor would as at this time have told us such things as these.

We have already commented on the contents of verse 22 under our remarks on 6:22. Of course, if Manoah had been a modern Charismatic, he would have written a book on how he saw God and how he was just filled with warmth and love and joy; and [he would have made a mint off a bunch of Christian suckers.]{.class-1}

[In that Book, when a man had an encounter with God, he fell on his face in terror. When Moses met God at the burning bush, he ]{.class-22}["hid his face"]{.class-27}[ because ]{.class-22}["he was afraid to look upon God"]{.class-27}[ (Exod. 3:6). Isaiah said, ]{.class-22}["Woe is me! for I am undone; because I am a man of unclean lips . . .]{.class-27}[ for mine eyes have seen the King, the LORD of hosts"]{.class-0} (Isa. 6:5). Saul of Tarsus was knocked off his horse to the ground (Acts 9:4). And when John saw Christ in His glory, he ["fell at his feet as dead"]{.class-0} (Rev. 1:17).

Do you see the gap between that Book and your average Christian bookstore? Brethren, when I get called up to the Judgment Seat of Christ, I am going to crawl in with my face to the floor, and I'm not going to look up until I'm told. And if you have any sense, you'll do the same.

Of course, it was not God's intention to kill Manoah and his wife; otherwise He wouldn't have accepted their sacrifice. When Aaron's two sons ["Nadab and Abihu" "offered strange fire"]{.class-0} to the Lord, God sent fire and dropped them dead (Lev. 10:1--2). When Korah and the men who followed him offered up incense to the Lord in rebellion, God burned them alive (Num. 16:16--35). Do you want God to spare you from the Lake of Fire?
